# Finance Foundations

> Finance exists in the real world. It impacts everyone: individuals, families, companies, and governments. While it may sound complicated, at its core finance is about deciding what to buy, getting the money you need to buy it, and efficiently managing the resources you already own. In this course, Brigham Young University business professors Jim and Kay Stice provide a comprehensive overview of fi

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241)

### 1. What Is Finance?

#### Finance inside of companies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=0)** - So what is finance?
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=2)** Finance broadly defined involves deciding what you need to buy, determining where you're going to get the money to buy those things, and then managing those things once you've bought them.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's drill down inside a company to learn how companies make decisions about financing and how those decisions impact company spending.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=22)** First, how do I decide what things I need?
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=25)** Well, there are long-term decisions that need to be made.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=28)** Do I need to buy some land?
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=30)** Do I need to buy some buildings?
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=32)** Do I need to buy machines?
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=34)** This long-term decision making process is part of finance.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=38)** Short-term finance decisions include deciding how much cash I need, how much inventory should I have on hand, should I let my customers buy on credit?
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=48)** Making these short-term decisions is also part of finance.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=52)** These short-term finance decisions also include deciding on operating items such as the appropriate number of staff, the right amount of research and development and marketing.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=63)** All these decisions, short term and long term, are issues of finance, and they all require money.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=71)** So that leads into the second area of finance inside the company, where do I get that money?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=77)** Do I borrow the money?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=78)** Do I ask shareholders or partners to pool their personal savings and put them in the company so that we can use that money to buy the things that we need?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=87)** Or do I use internally generated profits?
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=90)** And the question underlining the third major area of finance is this.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=95)** Once I have all those things and I've paid for them, how do I manage them?
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=100)** That's an issue of timing, scheduling, budgets, interfacing with my outside suppliers and my staff and so forth.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=109)** And as part of managing things inside my company, I also need to decide how to protect those things.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=116)** I need to have controls and procedures in place inside my company to effectively manage the things that I have.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=123)** Now, when most people say finance, they have a more narrow set of issues in mind than those I've just discussed.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=132)** They are only focusing on the second of the three broad issues, that second one, how do I get the money to buy the things that I need?
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=141)** And this issue of obtaining money applies on both sides.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=144)** The company seeking the money and the investors providing the money.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=149)** The companies seeking money consider whether to borrow the money from a bank or other lender, or to receive the money as an investment from partners or shareholders.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=159)** In whatever way the company gets the money, they have to get the money from somebody outside the company.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=166)** So those outsiders, do they want to invest in this company or do they want to invest in another company?
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=173)** And there are third parties involved, financial institutions that put these parties together.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=178)** One party needs to borrow money, another party wants to lend money, somebody has to put these two parties together.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=185)** So when most people talk about finance, realize that they are usually just focusing on this one narrow sliver of finance, the process of a company obtaining the money it needs to buy the things that it wants.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-inside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=199)** But you should realize that finance is much bigger than just this one issue.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), require (1), this. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - so (1)

#### Finance outside of companies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-outside-of-companies?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/finance-outside-of-companies?u=76281980&t=0)** - So, where does the company go to get the money it needs to purchase the things it wants?

### 2. Risk and Return

#### What is risk?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about risk.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=2)** First, what is risk?
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=4)** Risk is uncertainty about what will happen in the future.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=7)** In the future, there will be variability as to possible outcomes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=12)** When we make an investment in a product, we hire a key person, or we build a building, it could go well, it could go poorly.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=19)** Variability in potential outcomes is what we call risk.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=23)** Consider a real estate investment.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=26)** I can build a commercial real estate building and rent it out to tenants.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=30)** The building can be in the heart of the business district where there are many potential tenants.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=35)** I'm pretty confident that I'll be able to rent space in that building.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=39)** On the other hand, let's suppose I'm considering building a commercial building in an industrial park at the edge of town.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=45)** And when I say the edge of town, I mean it's out there where nobody is there yet.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=51)** There are weeds on the lot. Tenants may or may not come.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=54)** I'm not sure.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=56)** There is much more risk, much more variability in potential outcomes out there on the edge of town.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=63)** There are many different kinds of business risk.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=65)** Here are five.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=66)** Price risk, credit risk, interest rate risk, exchange rate risk, and cash flow risk.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=73)** Let's talk briefly about each one of these.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=76)** Price risk is the uncertainty about the future price of an asset.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=81)** I'm going to need to buy inventory in the future.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=83)** Will the price go up or down?
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=86)** I'm going to need to buy equipment in the future.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=88)** Will the price be higher or lower in the future?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=92)** Now credit risk is the uncertainty that the party on the other side of the agreement will abide by the terms of the agreement.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=99)** I make a sale on credit to a customer.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=101)** Will they pay or not?
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=102)** There is a slight chance they won't pay.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=105)** That is credit risk.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=108)** Interest rate risk is the uncertainty about future interest rates and their impact on my cash flows.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=113)** For example, let's suppose I have a variable interest rate loan.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=118)** Rates go up or down.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=120)** If the rate goes up in the future, that's going to require increased payments and more cash outflow for me.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=127)** Exchange rate risk is the uncertainty about fair values and future cash flows arising when assets and liabilities are denominated in a foreign currency.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=137)** Let's suppose I have a receivable from a company in France.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=141)** The receivable is denominated in Euros.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=145)** Because the receivable is denominated in Euros, and if I plan to convert those Euros into US dollars, I'm not sure how many US dollars I will receive in the future.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=156)** Cash flow risk is the uncertainty about the amount of cash that's going to be generated or consumed in the future based on decisions that I make today.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=166)** I buy a piece of equipment assuming that it's going to generate cost savings in the future, but I'm not sure what those cost savings are going to be.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=174)** That is cash flow risk.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=177)** Some risk is an unwanted side effect of doing business.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=180)** For example, if I'm in the airline business, a big risk is the uncertain cost of jet fuel.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=187)** Because I'm in the airline industry, I am subject to price risk related to fuel prices.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=193)** On the other hand, there are companies who are in the existence for the very purpose of helping individuals and other companies manage risk.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=201)** For example, insurance companies exist because of risk.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=206)** It is their job to take risk from me and assume it themselves, and I'll pay for that.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/what-is-risk?u=76281980&t=212)** So there are many different types of risks associated with business, and there are companies that will help me mitigate those risks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (5), require (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **Analogies:** for example (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - let (1)

#### Why we don’t like risk

#### Reducing risk through diversification
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=0)** - We don't like risk.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=2)** We have to be paid to bear certain types of risks.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, some risks can be reduced through diversification.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=9)** Diversification is just a fancy way of saying don't put all your eggs in one basket.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=14)** If you put all your eggs in one basket and you trip, you've ruined all your eggs, but if you spread your eggs around, you've diversified your risk.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=22)** Professor Harry Markowitz won a Nobel Prize for mathematically proving this statement, don't put all your eggs in one basket.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=32)** It's a way to reduce risk by spreading your risk around.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=35)** Now, some risks are avoidable and some risks are unavoidable.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=41)** For example, think about job risk.
>

> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=57)** I'm not going to put my hard hat on."

> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=60)** You're not going to get paid for being stupid and working without your hard hat.

> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=64)** You don't get paid extra for bearing additional risk when that risk is avoidable, as it would be in this case.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=70)** Don't expect to be paid extra for bearing risk that can be avoided.

>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=151)** That's extra risk, but you're not going to be paid for bearing risk that can be eliminated or reduced through diversification.

>

> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=199)** They don't put all their eggs in one basket.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=202)** If I invest all of my money in one company, then I'm exposed to all kinds of risks from random good and bad events, but if I spread my money out among 30 different companies, then the random bad things in one company will be balanced out by random good things in another company.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=221)** I can spread out or reduce my risk through diversification.

> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=228)** Diversification reduces risk.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reducing-risk-through-diversification?u=76281980&t=231)** Companies don't get compensated for bearing risk that can be eliminated or reduced through diversification.

#### Beta: The concept
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=0)** - Risk.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=2)** Now in business, it's everywhere.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=4)** You can work to reduce risk or to estimate the price you'd have to pay to get someone to accept risk, but there's some risks that you really can't do anything about.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=14)** For example, during 2008 and 2009, there was a global economic recession.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=20)** Everyone in the world was impacted.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=21)** There was no way to avoid that risk.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=25)** A risk that you can't do anything about is called beta risk.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=29)** Beta measures the market-wide risk that cannot be eliminated through diversification because it happens to everybody.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=37)** To get us into measuring this type of risk, it's time to talk about what is probably the most famous finance model, the capital asset pricing model, or the CAPM.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=48)** Now, rather than jump right into the CAPM, we will first examine its pieces, and then once we understand the pieces, we'll put them all together.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=58)** This famous model starts with some simple insights.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=62)** We know that investors are risk-averse, so you have to compensate them to get them to accept risk.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=70)** We also know that some risk can be eliminated through diversification, but there are some risks that cannot be eliminated through diversification.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=79)** That's beta risk.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=81)** Now, beta computationally is the, and get ready for this, is the co-variance between a company's returns and the market returns divided by the variance in the market returns, ouch.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=93)** My head just started to hurt.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=96)** Now that mathematical statistical statement seems scary, but the idea is easy.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=101)** Beta measures the investment risk that cannot be eliminated through diversification.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=107)** And here's a critical point.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=108)** If you can't eliminate the risk, it needs to be priced.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=112)** In other words, you should expect a higher return for assuming that risk.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=117)** Beta helps you in measuring that higher expected return.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=121)** Now, let me give you an example.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=123)** Cash has a beta of zero, and here's what that means.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=127)** Imagine a dollar bill.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=129)** Ignore for a moment the impact of inflation and the impact of foreign currency fluctuations.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=134)** A dollar bill is a dollar bill in this simple example.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=138)** If the economy is strong this year, dollar bill is still a dollar bill.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=142)** It's worth a dollar.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=143)** If the economy is weak this year, dollar bill is still worth a dollar.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=148)** The investment in that dollar bill is immune to fluctuations in broad economic forces.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=154)** Cash has a beta of zero.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=157)** Now, let's assume that you have a broad investment portfolio, bought a little bit of this, little bit of that.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=164)** I own lots of companies in the economy.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=167)** If the market goes up 15%, my broad investments will go up about 15%.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=172)** If the market goes down by 10%, my investment portfolio value will go down by about 10%.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=179)** This means that my investment portfolio, my collection of investments, has a beta of one.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=186)** Now, what would it mean for a company to have a beta of more than one?
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=190)** Well, I'll give you an example.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=191)** The home building industry has a beta of more than one.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=196)** When the economy gets hot, home builders get really hot, red hot.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=201)** When the economy cools, the home building industry gets downright cold.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=206)** Think about your local community.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=208)** When the local economy is doing well, then houses start going up all over the place.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=213)** On the other hand, when the local economy goes down just a little bit, you see activity at all those construction sites slow down.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=221)** Little movements in the broader economy result in big movements in the home building industry.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=227)** That's reflected mathematically in the fact that those in the home building industry have a beta of more than one.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=234)** How to compute beta?
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=235)** Best to let others do that.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=238)** There are database is available on the internet measuring beta for most large companies.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=243)** Let's let the internet do the heavy computational lifting.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-the-concept?u=76281980&t=248)** No need for us to do the intricate computations, but remember, beta measures market-wide risk that you cannot manage and you can't get rid of, and it's used as one important input in measuring an investment's expected return.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Env Vars:** capm (2)

#### Beta: Examples
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's discuss some real beta examples.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=4)** Here you see a list of companies with which we are all familiar, along with their associated betas.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=10)** Consider Ford Motor Company.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=12)** If you are fearful of your job, if the economy is going down, and you're fearful of a recession, then you are not going to run out and buy a new car.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=23)** So when there's a little downturn in the economy, Ford Motor sales go down substantially.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=29)** But when the economy goes back up, lots of people want to buy cars.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=34)** They've got this pent up demand.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=36)** So Ford Motor Company sales go way up.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=39)** The same pattern is seen with General Motors and even Tesla.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=43)** Their betas are substantially greater than one.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=46)** Consider Bank of America.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=48)** Banks, especially large banks, sometimes engage in risky behavior.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=55)** They're selling derivatives, they're trading derivatives, and collateralized debt instruments, and mortgage backed securities, which all sounds very exotic and scary.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=64)** And it is scary because of what it has done.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=67)** When the broad economy is doing well, large banks, such as Bank of America, do great.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=73)** When the economy goes down, these banks get killed, they get hammered.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=79)** They have a large beta risk.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=81)** The same is true for more broad-based financial services firms such as Morgan Stanley.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=87)** Now, let's consider companies with beta around one.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=92)** Alphabet, parent company of Google, Microsoft, and Apple all have betas around one.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=100)** This reflects the broad importance of technology in our economy.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=104)** The economic prospects of these technology companies pretty much track the economy.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=110)** When the economy is strong, these companies are strong.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=113)** When the economy goes down a little bit, they go down a little bit.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=116)** They have betas around one.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=118)** Now, what does it mean for a company to have a beta that's quite low?
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=123)** Well, McDonald's, Walmart, Costco, and Coca-Cola have betas that are quite low, because even when the economy is down you need to buy clothes, you need to buy groceries, you need fast food, and you need something to drink.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=138)** When the economy goes up, you don't all of a sudden decide that you need five Big Macs a day, one Big Mac will probably still be enough.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=146)** So the fluctuations of performance of Walmart, McDonald's, Costco, and Coke are almost independent of what happens in the broad economy.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=156)** One other industry that is almost immune from broad economic effects is Altria.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=163)** You may know Altria by its famous brand name Marlboro.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=167)** They sell cigarettes.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=168)** Even when the economy is down, smokers still need to buy cigarettes.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=173)** When the economy is up, they're still buying about the same number of cigarettes.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=177)** To summarize, a company's beta tells us how that company's stock price moves as the overall stock market moves.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/beta-examples?u=76281980&t=185)** High betas, high swings, low betas, not so much.

#### Risk-free rate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=0)** - The risk-free rate is another component of the famous capital asset pricing model.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=7)** The risk-free rate represents the return an investor would expect from an absolutely risk-free investment over a specific period of time.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=17)** The risk-free rate is useful in helping a potential investor compute how much return to expect for a given level of risk.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=25)** In other words, for an investment option bearing no risk, there's an expected rate of return, the risk-free rate.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=31)** For a given level of risk, how much return over and above the risk-free rate would an investor require?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=39)** The risk-free rate is the starting point in this computation.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=43)** Adding more risk results in a higher expected rate of return.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=47)** The risk-free rate is the baseline.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=50)** Let's imagine that you've been asked to loan $1 to the most trustworthy organization on earth.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=56)** I don't know who that is, but let's just imagine them.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=59)** The most trustworthy organization on earth.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=62)** They're going to pay us back for sure.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=65)** So what interest rate would I charge on this loan to the most trustworthy organization?
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=71)** Well that's the risk-free loan, that's the risk-free rate.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=74)** Well the interest rate is not going to be zero for a couple of reasons.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=77)** First of all, there might be some expected inflation.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=80)** Price levels might be expected to go up two, three, four, 5% over the next year.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=86)** So I have to build at least that expected inflation into the interest rate that I'm going to charge.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=93)** In addition, there's the time value of money.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=95)** A dollar now is worth more than a dollar a year from now.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=99)** So if I'm going to lend you a dollar now, you had better pay me back a little bit more than that a year from now, even if there's no risk.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=108)** So the risk-free rate on an investment is going to be a combination of two things, the general time value of money, and expected inflation.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=117)** Now the risk-free rate is not always the same over time, and it's not always the same in each individual country.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=123)** The risk-free rates will depend on what the expected inflation rate is forecasted to be.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=129)** But over the last 50, 60, 70 years worldwide, the risk-free rate has been about 5%.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=135)** Now that's just an approximation, but 5%, keep that number in your mind.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=140)** When inflation is low, the risk-free rate is going to be less than 5%.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=145)** When inflation is high, the risk-free rate is going to be more than 5%.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=149)** For example, when my wife and I first got married, the risk-free rate was about 16% because we were living in the United States at the time, and inflation was high, about 13% back then.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=161)** Now over the last 10 years in the United States, the risk-free rate has averaged about 2.5%, but on average, over a long period of time, the risk-free rate is about 5%.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=174)** Not always 5%, but that's just a good number, a benchmark to keep in mind.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=179)** Now what are we using the risk-free rate for?
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=181)** It serves as the benchmark for an expected return involving no risk.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/risk-free-rate?u=76281980&t=186)** When we start to price risk into our expected return, the premium reflected in that price is the increment over and above the risk-free rate.

#### Equity risk premium
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=0)** - Another important element of the Capital Asset Pricing Model is something called the equity risk premium.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=7)** In this context, equity means an investment in the ownership shares of a company.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=12)** Equity stock investments are what we're talking about, and the simple insight is this, an investment in the shares of a company is more risky than say, an investment in a bank account, so there should be a higher rate of return.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=26)** If you invest in a bank account, there's almost no risk at all, so the return that you're going to earn is low, but if you're going to invest in stocks, there is much more risk, so you'll expect a higher return.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=38)** We call this the equity risk premium.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=42)** The equity risk premium is the extra amount you expect to earn on your investment because you're investing in risky assets.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=48)** In the United States, for example, over the last 50, 60, 70 years, an investment in stocks has averaged a return of about 10 or 11% per year.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=59)** The historical rate of return expected on a risk-free investment is about 5%.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=64)** The difference between these two, the 5% risk-free rate, and the 11% rate on an equity investments, that is the equity risk premium.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=75)** The equity risk premium, over time, the United States has been about 6%, that difference.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=80)** That's the extra amount that you're going to earn by being willing to accept the risk of investing in stocks.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=85)** That is, equity investments.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=88)** Now, there are complexities.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=90)** Are we talking about equity investments in small companies or large companies?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=95)** And when we're talking about the risk-free rate, are we talking about short-term rates or long-term rates?
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=100)** What exactly are we talking about?
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=103)** Those complexities can get sidetracked from our main objective, that main objective being to try and price risk to ensure an adequate expected return.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=113)** Let's just leave those interesting complexities to the side.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=118)** We know enough.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=119)** For you and I, let's just remember the two historical numbers, the risk-free rate, about 5%, and the equity risk premium about 6%.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/equity-risk-premium?u=76281980&t=129)** These numbers will assist us in evaluating the expected returns of alternative investments, and evaluating alternative investments is at the heart of corporate finance.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** return. (3), let (2), this, (1)
> **Definitions:** we call this (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - another (1)

#### Capital asset pricing model (CAPM)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=0)** - We are now standing at the center of much of corporate finance, evaluating the trade-off between anticipated risk and expected return.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=10)** Again, more anticipated risk means more expected return.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=15)** And at the center of corporate finance, we see the Capital Asset Pricing Model.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=20)** To this point, we have looked at all the pieces.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=22)** We are now ready to look at the entire capital asset pricey model.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=27)** Here is the Capital Asset Pricing Model, the CAPM in all its glory.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=33)** We've discussed all of these components, now we will tie them all together.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=38)** Remember, the risk-free rate is the minimum return that we have to earn on any investment.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=43)** Returns on investments in the stocks of companies have to be higher than the risk-free rate.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=48)** We call that difference the equity risk premium.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=51)** Okay, but how much higher?
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=53)** Well, that depends on the risk associated with the investment.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=56)** And remember, this is unavoidable risk or the beta risk.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=60)** Some risks can be avoided through diversification, but the beta risk cannot be avoided through diversification because it relates to overall movements in the economy.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=71)** Let's see how the Capital Asset Pricing Model works with some numbers.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=75)** Let's use General Motors to start.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=77)** Remember, if you want to induce me to invest in General Motors, you have to at least make me think that I'm going to earn the risk-free rate, 5%.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=86)** In addition, there needs to be an equity risk premium.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=90)** We're starting with the standard equity risk premium, 6% we'll assume, but General Motors, as we recall, has a high beta because General Motors is very susceptible to economy-wide movements.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=102)** 1.41 is the beta for General Motors.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=107)** So the total equity risk premium is the standard risk premium, 6%, multiplied by 1.41, that's 8.5%.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=117)** If you want me to invest in General Motors, you need to convince me that the investment is going to earn at least 5%, that's the risk-free rate, plus another 8.5% equity risk premium, 13.5%.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=131)** If I don't think I can earn 13.5% in an investment in General Motors, I'm not going to make the investment.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=137)** That's the insight from the Capital Asset Pricing Model.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=141)** How about Walmart?
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=142)** Walmart has low beta risk.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=144)** Walmart's economic circumstances are almost independent of overall economic movements in the economy, so the company has low beta risk.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=154)** If you want me to invest in Walmart, I have to first expect a risk-free return of 5%, then the equity risk premium, which in this case is going to be quite low.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=165)** The equity risk premium standard amount, 6%, multiplied by Walmart's 0.52 beta, so I only need to expect an equity risk premium with respect to Walmart of 3.1%, 5% risk-free rate plus 3.1% equity risk premium gives a total required return for an investment in Walmart stock of 8.1%.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=190)** Now, you might think that these computations seem pretty simple and that nobody would actually use this.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=196)** Well, I was in a courtroom for two weeks a few years ago as an expert witness.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=202)** I sat there for two weeks arguing about a court case involving tens of millions of dollars, and this involved the entire time arguing about which numbers to plug into the Capital Asset Pricing Model.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=216)** Do real people actually use the Capital Asset Pricing Model in estimating interest rates?
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=221)** Yes, they do.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=223)** According to the Capital Asset Pricing Model, the return that a person must expect in order to get her or him to make an investment is the risk-free rate plus an equity risk premium, which is based on the company's beta.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-asset-pricing-model-capm?u=76281980&t=238)** And now that you know the Capital Asset Pricing Model, the CAPM, try it out on your friends the next time you go to a party.

### 3. Understanding Financial Statements

#### Overview of financial statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=0)** - Finance is identifying necessary resources, figuring out how to get the money to buy those resources, and then managing those resources efficiently once you have them.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=11)** - To identify and determine and manage resources, you need information.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=16)** - One source of that information is a company's financial statements: the balance sheet, the income statement, and the statement of cash flows.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=23)** - Let's define the items found in these three financial statements.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=26)** - [Presenter] The first primary financial statement is the balance sheet.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=30)** A balance sheet is a listing of a company's valuable resources, its assets. - An asset is defined as a tangible thing or an intangible right that a company owns, or controls, that is expected to generate benefits in the future.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=44)** - [Presenter] For example, as of January 31, 2024, Walmart had total assets of $252 billion.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=54)** Of this amount, 55 billion was the cost of inventory sitting on the shelves of the 10,616 Walmart stores around the world.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=64)** Most of the rest of the $252 billion in assets was in the form of land, buildings and the shelves, display cases, and other equipment in the stores.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=72)** - A balance sheet also shows the three general sources that companies use to get money to buy assets.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=78)** First, they can borrow it.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=79)** If you borrow money, then of course, you got to pay it back.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=83)** - [Presenter] This obligation to repay an amount borrowed is called a liability.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=87)** - The second source for money to buy assets is that owners can take money from their private savings and invest it in the business.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=94)** - And the third source is that the company can generate profits, which belong to the owners, that are then kept in the business to buy more assets.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=102)** - [Presenter] Together, these two sources of financing, owner direct investment and retained profits, those two together are called equity.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=109)** Equity is the amount of total financing provided by the owners.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=113)** - Assets, liabilities, and equity, those are the three items found in the balance sheet.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=119)** - [Presenter] The second primary financial statement is the income statement.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=122)** An income statement is a report telling you how much money a company made during the month or the quarter or the year, whatever period is covered by the income statement.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=130)** - The income statement contains revenues and expenses.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=133)** - [Presenter] The revenue amount is the amount of assets generated through doing business operations.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=139)** In 2023, Walmart generated $648 billion in assets, cash or credit/debit card electronic transfers, through the selling of stuff to you and to me.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=150)** - [Presenter] The expense amount is the amount of assets consumed in doing business operations.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=155)** - For example, during 2023, Walmart consumed $490 billion in paying suppliers for the inventory items sold to you and me.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=163)** Walmart has other expenses as well, like expenses related to rent and utilities and wages.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=170)** - [Presenter] And the difference between revenues and expenses is called net income.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=174)** Hopefully this difference is positive, meaning that the company's business operations have generated more assets than they've consumed.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=182)** - [Presenter] Now, the third primary financial statements is the statement of cash flows, which is a report of a company's cash receipts and cash payments, separated into three categories: operating, investing, and financing.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=195)** - Operating cash flows are the things that you do every day, collecting cash from customers, paying employees, and so forth.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=202)** - Investing cash flows are investments in the productive capacity of the business, buying more buildings and trucks and land and so forth.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=210)** - And financing activities involve getting the money to buy the stuff that I need through borrowing and through shareholder investment, and then repaying loans and paying dividends to shareholders.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=221)** - [Presenter] During 2023, Walmart's operations generated almost $36 billion in net cash.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=227)** Of this amount, $21 billion was used to buy new long-term assets, such as land and building.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=233)** And $6 billion was paid to shareholders as dividends.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=236)** - Now, what about the rest of the cash generated by Walmart in 2023?
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=240)** Well, Walmart used some of it to buy back shares of its own stock from its shareholders, but that's a story for another day.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/overview-of-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=247)** - So those are the three financial statements: the balance sheet, the income statement, and the statement of cash flows.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - [presenter] (10), - the (2), - and (2), - finance (1), - to (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (4), is called (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), such as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1), private (1)

#### The balance sheet
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about the balance sheet in a little bit more detail.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=3)** The balance sheet is built around one of the most awesome creations of the human mind, the accounting equation, assets equal liabilities plus equity.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=14)** The great insight behind this accounting equation is this; every time we get an asset, let's write down where we got the money to buy that asset.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=23)** So we've got the two sides of the accounting equation.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=26)** The first side, the asset side, that's the real world.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=29)** You can go out and touch a company's assets.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=32)** It's cash, it's buildings, it's land.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=34)** That's the real part of a company.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=37)** The other half of the accounting equation just says where you got the money to buy those assets.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=42)** So, what are assets?
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=44)** A formal definition is that assets are resources owned or controlled by a company that will provide probable future benefit.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=52)** Let's take a simple example, the balance sheet of Walmart.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=56)** As of January 31st, 2024, the balance sheet of Walmart says that the company has almost $10 billion in cash.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=65)** Is that a resource that will provide probable future benefit?
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=69)** I guess. Yes.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=70)** Cash is a good asset.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=73)** Inventory is the stuff on the Walmart shelves that you can buy from Walmart.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=77)** On January 31st, 2024, Walmart had inventory costing $55 billion.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=82)** Inventory's an asset.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=84)** Walmart's buildings, $112 billion.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=88)** With the additional assets such as the shelves and freezers in Walmart stores.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=93)** As of January 31st, 2024, the total amount reported for all the assets in Walmart's balance sheet was $252 billion.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=104)** For United Airlines, important assets are the airplanes.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=107)** For Disney, important assets are the theme parks.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=111)** Then the other side of Walmart's balance sheet outlines where Walmart got the money to buy that $252 billion in assets.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=119)** First are the liabilities, the amounts that Walmart used to buy assets, but will have to repay in the future.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=126)** As of January 31st, 2024, Walmart reported about $61 billion in loans.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=134)** Loans are a good source of money to buy assets.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=137)** Walmart also reported $57 billion in something called accounts payable.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=143)** Now, when Walmart buys inventory, the company promises its suppliers, Procter & Gamble, or Black+Decker, or whomever, they promised them that they will pay in the future.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=153)** That's a liability, an obligation to pay in the future.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=157)** The inside of the accounting equation is that you've got to write down the asset, the inventory in this case, and you also write down how you bought the asset.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=166)** In this case, you promised to pay later.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=168)** Accounts payable is a good example of a liability.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=173)** Another way to get money to buy assets is from the business owners.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=176)** In this case, the source of the financing is called equity.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=180)** Owners can invest directly in a business.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=183)** We call those amounts paid in capital or contributed capital, or in the case of a corporation, common stock.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=190)** Another way that owners invest in a business is through retained profits.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=195)** When a business generates a profit, the owners own those profits.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=199)** The owners can choose to take some of the profits out of the business for personal use.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=203)** We call those distributed profits, dividends.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=207)** In contrast, more often than not, owners will decide to put most of the profits back into the business for expansion.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=215)** If the profits are kept in the business, then the label we give to that source of equity is retained earnings.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=222)** Retained earnings is the largest single source of financing by Walmart amounting to almost $90 billion.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=229)** Retained earnings is the amount of profits generated by Walmart that have been kept by the company to purchase new assets.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=236)** This retained earnings amount has been accumulating since July of 1962 when the company started.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=243)** The sum of two things paid in capital and retained earnings is equal to equity.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=249)** Liabilities and equity are the labels for the two general sources of financing to buy assets.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=255)** So, where did Walmart get the $252 billion it needed to buy its assets?
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=261)** Well, a lot of it came from retained earnings, $90 billion.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=264)** Some of it came from loans.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=266)** Quite a bit came from accounts payable.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=269)** Now, the question that should come to your mind as you look at Walmart's liabilities and retained earnings is the following, is this the right mix?
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=277)** Should Walmart have borrowed a little more or a little less?
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-balance-sheet?u=76281980&t=281)** This question of financing mix is a key question of corporate finance that we'll address in detail later in this course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
