> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is called (1)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - let (1)

#### The income statement
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=0)** - The second primary financial statement is the income statement.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=4)** The income statement is the financial statement that people talk about all the time.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=8)** People naturally want to know how much money did the company make?
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=12)** The income statement contains two items, revenues and expenses.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=17)** Revenues minus expenses equal net income.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=20)** That's it. That's the income statement.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=23)** But what does the word revenue mean?
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=25)** The meaning is kind of subtle but important.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=28)** Revenue is the amount of assets created from the sale of goods or services.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=33)** Let's say that you have a hundred dollars bill.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=35)** That's an asset.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=36)** Now, where could you have obtained that asset?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=39)** Well, you could have borrowed it from somebody.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=41)** Then we call the source of that asset a liability, or you could have taken it from your personal savings, in which case we would call the source of that asset paid in capital, or you could have gone out and done some work and earned the $100.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=56)** If you generate the asset through your profitable efforts, then the source of the asset is revenue.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=63)** People often have difficulty mixing these two together, asset and revenue.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=68)** There is the asset, the $100, and there is the source of the asset.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=73)** Borrowing personal investment or work.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=76)** Revenue tells us that a company obtains some of its assets through work, through providing value to its customers.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=84)** So how do companies generate assets through business operations?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=87)** Well, that depends on what the business is.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=90)** Microsoft, for example, generates assets by collecting it from you and me through selling software, hardware, and services.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=100)** How does Walmart generate assets through doing business?
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=102)** Well, they sell products to you and me, and they sell us memberships in their Sam's Club.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=108)** So that's the way Walmart generates assets through doing business.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=111)** Those are Walmart's revenues.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=114)** Revenues are ways that companies generate assets through doing business.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=119)** And whatever your company is, that's the way you're going to generate revenue.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=124)** This differs depending on what industry you're in.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=127)** Net income is revenues, minus expenses.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=131)** As discussed revenues are the amount of assets generated through business operations in the same way expenses are the amount of assets consumed through business operations.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=142)** For example, for Microsoft in order to generate those revenues by selling software, hardware, and services to you and me, they have to pay programmers, they use equipment, they have to pay for electricity, they have to pay for maintenance on all their facilities.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=158)** Paying for those items consumes assets.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=160)** Those are expenses.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=163)** For Walmart, what's their major expense?
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=166)** Their major expense is the cost of the goods that they sell to you and me.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=170)** On average, if Walmart sells something to you and me for $100, that thing costs them about $76.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=178)** So that $76 is an expense, the amount of assets consumed in generating revenues.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=185)** Walmart also has buildings that slowly wear out over time.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=189)** The amount of that wear and tear represents assets being consumed in generating revenues.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=195)** We call that depreciation expense.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=198)** So expenses are defined as the amount of assets consumed in generating revenues.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=203)** Now, let's take a look at some simplified income statements for three companies, Walmart, Apple, and Tesla.
>

> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=260)** Expenses are the amount of assets consumed in doing business.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=265)** Net income, revenues minus expenses, is the net amount of assets generated in doing business.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-income-statement?u=76281980&t=271)** Turns out it's as simple as that.

#### The statement of cash flows
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=0)** - The statement of cash flows is the baby of the financial statements.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=4)** The balance sheet and the income statement, they've been around for over 500 years.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=9)** In contrast, the statement of cash flows first started appearing in just 1988.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=14)** So it's brand new in an accounting sense.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, as we talk about the statement of cash flows, you're going to wonder why accountants didn't invent it sooner.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=23)** I ask myself the same question.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=25)** The idea behind the statement of cash flows is so simple.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=29)** The statement of cash flows is a report of the amount of cash collected and cash paid by a company during the period.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=36)** Either a month, a quarter, or a year.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=39)** That's a pretty easy concept, we're just focusing on cash.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=43)** How much cash did you collect? How much cash did you pay?
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=47)** The structure of the statement of cash flows is to separate the cash flows into three categories.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=52)** Operating activities, investing activities, and financing activities.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=59)** Operating activities are the things that you do every single day, your operations.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=64)** When would you collect cash from operating activities?
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=68)** Walmart collects cash from its operating activities when it collects cash from selling items to you and me.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=75)** A consulting company collects cash by providing consulting services to you and me, and then collecting cash from us.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=82)** Those are cash inflows from operating activities.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=85)** Examples of cash outflows from operating activities are cash paid for wages, for utilities, for taxes, for interest.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=94)** Operating activities are the things that a company does every single day.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=100)** The next category of cash flows is investing activities.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=103)** Here, the word investing means investing in the productive capacity of the business.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=109)** So cash outflows from investing activities are buying new buildings, buying new land, buying new machines.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=116)** Now, how often do I do investing activities?
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=119)** Well, occasionally, but not every day.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=121)** These are not routine things.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=123)** I don't go out and buy a building for my business every single day.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=127)** The third category of cash flows is financing activities.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=131)** Financing activities are just what they sound like.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=133)** I'm getting the cash to pay for the necessary things in my business.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=137)** I'm borrowing some money.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=138)** I'm getting new investment from owners.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=141)** Those are cash inflows from financing activities.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=144)** Cash outflows from financing activities, well, I'm repaying those loans.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=149)** I'm paying dividends to owners.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=151)** Those are cash outflows from financing activities.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=154)** How often do I do financing activities?
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=157)** Well, occasionally, but not very often.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=160)** Operating activities, those are the things that I do every single day repetitively, over and over.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=166)** I'm in business to conduct operations, that is what I exist for.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=171)** Investing activities are expanding the productive capacity of my business.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=176)** Financing activities are getting the cash to do what I need to do.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=180)** I'm borrowing money and I'm paying it back.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=182)** I'm getting cash from shareholders and then I'm paying dividends to them.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=186)** Cash from operating activities, investing activities, and financing activities.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=192)** Let's look at some cash flow numbers for five companies about which you may have heard.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=197)** Microsoft, Apple, ExxonMobil, Walmart, and Tesla.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=202)** These numbers are for 2024, and are in billions of US dollars.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=208)** Each of these companies had positive cash flows from operating activities.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=213)** But companies then need to ensure that they maintain and perhaps expand their productive capacity.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=219)** So a key investing activity is spending cash to buy new land, new buildings, new equipment, and new trucks.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=225)** That's called capital expenditures, or CapEx.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=229)** If you subtract cash paid for CapEx from cash from operating activities, you get a number commonly called free cash flow.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=238)** Now, free cash flow is a very interesting name because the name tells you exactly what it is.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=245)** It's the cash that the company is free to use in whatever way seems desirable.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=250)** The amount of free cash flow represents company's financial flexibility.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=255)** So what did Apple, for example, do with its free cash flow?
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=258)** For the most part, they paid dividends and they bought back shares of stock from their own shareholders.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=265)** Remember that the key number in the statement of cash flows is cash from operating activities.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=270)** If a company is generating positive cash from their operating activities, the things they do every single day, that's a good sign.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=277)** If that number is negative, there are some problems.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=281)** Well, that's the statement of cash flows.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-statement-of-cash-flows?u=76281980&t=283)** It's been around for about 35 years, but it has been an awesome 35 years.

> **Code Keywords:** for. (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Widely-used ratios: Debt ratio and current ratio
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=0)** - You can get a lot of information from the financial statements just by doing a simple thing: divide one accounting number by another.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=8)** The results of these calculations are called financial ratios.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=12)** Now, of course, there are hundreds of possible financial ratios, but here, we will discuss just six key financial ratios.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=20)** We will learn about the debt ratio, the current ratio, return on sales, asset turnover, return on equity, and price earnings ratio.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=29)** Now, debt ratio is computed as total liabilities divided by total assets.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=35)** Debt ratio is a measure of a company's leverage.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=39)** Leverage is a common way to describe the degree to which a company has borrowed the money needed to buy its assets, compared to getting that money as investments from owners.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=50)** For Walmart, for example, as of January 31st, 2024, the $162 billion of total liabilities relative to its $252 billion in total assets means the company borrowed about 64% of all the money it needed to buy its assets.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=69)** Now, where did Walmart get the other 36%?
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=73)** That 36% was invested by shareholders.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=77)** Walmart's debt ratio of 64% tells you the extent to which Walmart has financed its business through borrowing.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=86)** By comparison, Target has borrowed 76% of all the money it needed to buy its assets.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=94)** A general rule for large companies around the world is that debt ratios are usually between 50 and 60%, so we see that both Walmart and Target have a bit more leverage than average.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, if leverage gets too high, lenders become nervous about the borrower's ability to repay its loans.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=115)** Now, in the case of these two companies, their collateral, buildings, and inventory are quite secure, allowing them to safely borrow more.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=125)** Debt ratio, that's total liabilities divided by total assets, is a measure of leverage.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=131)** A second widely used ratio is the current ratio.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=135)** Current ratio is a measure of a company's liquidity, which means the ability of the company to pay its obligations in the short term.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=143)** Current ratio is computed as current assets divided by current liabilities.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=149)** Now, I think we should pause here to learn what a current asset is and what a current liability is.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=155)** A current asset is an asset that you expect to use within the coming year.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=160)** Cash, for example, is a current asset.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=163)** Specific dollars are not going to sit there for a year.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=166)** You're going to use them, circulate them, and replace them.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=169)** Inventory is also a current asset.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=172)** Hopefully, you're going to sell and then replace specific items of inventory within the coming year.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=178)** Now, current liabilities are obligations that you expect to pay within the coming year.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=183)** For example, you expect to pay your suppliers within 30 to 40 days, so accounts payable, it's a current liability.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=191)** You expect to pay your employees soon, so wages payable is a current liability.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=197)** The current ratio for Walmart here is 0.83.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=200)** That's $76.9 billion of current assets divided by $92.4 billion of current liabilities.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=208)** Now, let's stop for a second because that 0.83 should make you a little nervous.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=214)** Now, I'm going to explain to you in just a minute that we don't need to be worried about Walmart, but let's be nervous for just a moment because Walmart doesn't have enough current assets to be able to pay all of its current liabilities today.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=227)** It's kind of scary, but of course, Walmart doesn't actually have to stop today and pay all of its current liabilities with its existing current assets.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=237)** How does Walmart pay its obligations on an ongoing basis?
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=240)** With the cash flow that comes in every day.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=244)** Walmart has a current ratio that's less than one, but that is not a concern because of Walmart's stable and reliable operating cash cashflow.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=253)** Historically, banks like to see less stable companies have current ratios of two or higher.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=260)** That means if you had to pay all your current liabilities today with your current assets, you'd have a cushion of more than two to one.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-debt-ratio-and-current-ratio?u=76281980&t=268)** The current ratio is a measure of a company's liquidity, its ability to pay its obligations in the short term.

#### Return on sales, asset turnover, and return on equity
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=0)** - Another common financial ratio is return on sales.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=4)** Return on sales is a measure of a company's profitability.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=8)** Return on sales is computed as net income divided by total revenue.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=13)** For Walmart, return on sales is 2%, computed as $15.5 billion of net income divided by $648.1 billion in revenue.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=25)** This means that if Walmart has sales of $100, the shareholders of Walmart get to keep $2 after all the expenses, including interest and income taxes, have been paid.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=36)** This return on sales number is also sometimes called the profit margin.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=41)** By comparison, Target's profit margin is a robust 6%.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=46)** Now, if we had the time, we could do an interesting item by item comparison of the Walmart and Target income statements to see why Walmart's return on sales is just half as large as Target's.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=59)** For a high tech company, such as Google or Apple or Microsoft, return on sales is often over 20%.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=67)** The sales of high tech businesses can be very profitable.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=71)** Businesses like Target and Walmart that sell groceries and commodities have very thin margins.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=78)** We gain interesting insight into company profitability just by doing the simple computation of return on sales.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=85)** Now, another commonly used financial ratio is asset turnover.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=89)** Asset turnover is a measure of efficiency and is computed as sales divided by total assets.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=97)** For Walmart asset turnover is 2.6, which means that for every dollar of assets, Walmart is able to generate $2.60 of sales each year.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=108)** The higher the asset turnover number, the more efficient the company is at using its assets to generate sales.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=116)** For Target, asset turnover is just 1.9.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=120)** Now, let me illustrate the difference between these two companies, Walmart and Target, in terms of efficiency.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=127)** If I gave Walmart $100 in assets and I gave the same $100 in assets to Target, Walmart would be able to use the $100 in assets to generate an additional $70 in sales each year.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=142)** You see, the importance of efficiency is measured here by asset turnover.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=147)** Another widely used financial ratio, perhaps the most famous financial ratio, is return on equity, called ROE by its friends.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=157)** ROE is a measure of the overall profitability of a shareholder investment and is computed as net income divided by equity.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=166)** For Walmart, ROE is 17%, computed as $15.5 billion in net income divided by $90.3 billion in equity.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=177)** Remember that the equity number in the balance sheet is the amount invested in Walmart by the company's shareholders.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=184)** So for the year end of January 31st, 2024, Walmart shareholders earned 17% on their investment.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=192)** Target shareholders had a return on equity of 51%.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=196)** That's a huge number.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=199)** The shareholders of Target were much happier in this year than were the shareholders of Walmart, because the Target shareholders earned 51% on their investment, whereas the Walmart shareholders earned just 17%.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=214)** As a general rule, most companies have return on equity somewhere between 10 and 20%.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=220)** We see that Walmart's in this normal range.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=223)** If you see a company with a return on equity more than 20%, that company is awesome.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/return-on-sales-asset-turnover-and-return-on-equity?u=76281980&t=229)** If you see a company with a return on equity of less than 10%, well, that's not so good.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Versions:** 15.5 (2), 648.1 (1), 2.6 (1), 2.60 (1), 1.9 (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (4), means that (2)
> **Env Vars:** roe (3)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Best Practices:** general rule (1)
> **Speakers:** - another (1)

#### Widely-used ratios: Price-earnings ratio
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=0)** - The final widely used financial ratio that we'll talk about here is the price earnings ratio.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=6)** Now note earnings means the same thing as net income.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=10)** Price earnings ratio reflects how valuable a company's current earnings are.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=15)** This is an important idea because when you buy a company, you're not buying the past history of that company.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=22)** You're buying the future of that company.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=25)** Net income last year is relevant to the value of a company to the extent that it provides an indication that the company has processes and business models in place that are generating profits.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=36)** From the standpoint of an investor, your concern is whether that profitability is going to grow or shrink in the future.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=45)** This expected future earnings growth is what is reflected in the price earnings ratio.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=51)** The price earnings ratio is computed as the market value of a company's shares divided by the company's net income market.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=59)** Value of a company's shares is sometimes called the market capitalization.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=63)** This is the amount you would have to pay to buy 100% ownership of a company.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=69)** In early 2025, if you wanted to buy all the shares of Walmart, you would've had to pay $743 billion.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=77)** If you had some extra money to spend, you could buy Apple for $3.36 trillion.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=83)** Note, Apple's the first public company in history to exceed $3 trillion in market capitalization.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=90)** Now with a net income of $15.5 billion, the price earnings ratio for Walmart is 48.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=98)** This reflects the market's expectation of the future growth in earnings by Walmart.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=104)** By comparison, Target has a price earnings ratio of nine.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=109)** Each dollar of earnings of Walmart is more valuable than every dollar of earnings than Target.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=114)** And why is that?
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=116)** Remember that this computation is based on past earnings, and when you buy a company, you're buying its future.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=123)** So what investors are saying is that Walmart's earnings may not be that great right now, but we expect them to grow substantially in the future.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=132)** Typically, most companies have price earnings ratios between 10 and 30, and we see that Walmart and Target are both just outside that range in opposite directions.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=144)** A price earnings ratio of less than 10 means the market expects your company to grow very slowly in the future.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=151)** Target falls into this category.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=153)** A price earnings ratio of more than 30 means that the market expects your earnings to grow very rapidly in the future, and Walmart falls into this category.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=163)** Remember also that price earnings ratios or PE ratios change daily as the market value of a company changes.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=170)** So tomorrow, both Target and Walmart's PE ratios will be different.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=175)** Now, PE ratios are most meaningful through the trends they project rather than their value at one point in time.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=184)** In the course of teaching various cases over the years, the highest price earnings ratio I've ever seen is 2,220, indicating stratospheric expectations that never came to be.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=198)** Expectations about future earnings growth.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/widely-used-ratios-price-earnings-ratio?u=76281980&t=201)** Now, these six financial ratios discussed in this section certainly are not all the financial ratios that can be computed, but this selection of six shows you the usefulness of this simple procedure dividing one financial statement number by another.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Versions:** 3.36 (1), 15.5 (1)
> **Code Keywords:** public (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Forecasting financial statements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now, a common criticism I hear about accounting is it's too focused on the past.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=6)** Managers, bankers and investors don't want to know about the past.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=10)** They want to know about the future.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=12)** People say that looking at financial statements is like trying to drive your car by looking in the rear view mirror to see where you've been.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=19)** But I'm going to go with Winston Churchill on this topic.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=22)** He said, "The further back you can look, the further forward you are likely to see."
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=28)** Churchill, a student of history, understood that studying the past allows one an educated glimpse into the uncertain future.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=36)** Now, studying and analyzing the historical performance of a company allows financial statement users to project the future performance of a company.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=45)** We can make some reasonable assumption about a company's future performance, and it is then fairly straightforward to see what future financial statements might look like.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=54)** To illustrate, let's discuss forecasting a company's income statement.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=59)** The same general concept apply if we were looking at the balance sheet.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=63)** To begin, we recognize the amount of some expenses are directly tied to the amount of sales for the year.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=70)** For example, if we do nothing differently in the future, it seems reasonable to predict that the cost of sales will increase at the same rate as sales increase.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=81)** For example, year after year after year, Walmart's cost of sales is approximately 75% of Walmart's sales amount.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=90)** In other words, if Walmart sells you something for $100, that thing costs Walmart about $75 to purchase from its suppliers.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=99)** So if Walmart's sales are forecasted to increase next year to $1 trillion, that's a big number, then costs of sales will probably be about $750 billion.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=111)** Similarly, other operating expenses such as wages are also likely to maintain a constant relationship with the level of sales.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=120)** But some expenses don't necessarily vary with sales.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=124)** For example, depreciation expense will vary with the amount of a company's buildings and equipment.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=131)** If the amount of buildings and equipment increases, it's reasonable to expect depreciation expense to increase as well.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=138)** Now, consider interest expense.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=141)** The amount of interest expense is not tied to the level of sales.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=145)** Instead, you pay more interest if you have more loans.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=149)** If the amount of a company's loans goes up, it's reasonable to forecast that interest expense will also go up.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=155)** And let's not forget about income tax expense.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=159)** Income tax expense is not a function of sales.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=162)** It's a function of income.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=164)** Hence the name.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=165)** If you tell me forecasted income for next year, I can then apply the expected income tax rate to generate an estimate of next year's income tax expense.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=175)** Now, this may sound complicated, but it really isn't.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=179)** In creating forecasted financial statements, we just systematically think about what causes financial statement numbers to change, what events or activities drive those changes.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=190)** And then we forecast the future based on our expectations about those changing activities.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=197)** Using historical information and making some reasonable assumptions, we can get a glimpse as to what the future might look like.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=205)** And as we put more investigative effort into our assumptions, we can get an even more accurate picture of what the future might look like.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=213)** It's not hard and it's kind of fun.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=215)** And the nice thing about getting a glimpse of what the future looks like is that if you don't like it, you can change the assumptions associated with your forecast.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=225)** And then you need to make sure that those changed assumptions are associated with reasonable and achievable actions in the real world.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=233)** If they are, you can create a different financial future.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=238)** Financial statements are about the past, and they are also about the future.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=243)** As the old saying goes, if you keep doing what you're doing, you'll keep getting what you're getting.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=248)** So if you don't like what you're getting, you need to do something different.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/forecasting-financial-statements?u=76281980&t=252)** Forecasting financial statements can give us a glimpse into what we might achieve as a company if we do certain things differently.

> **Code Keywords:** let (2), function (2)

### 4. Short-Term Financial Management

#### Introducing short-term financial management
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=0)** - Nike makes athletic shoes and other sportswear.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=3)** Nike manufactures these items and then sells them to retail outlets, typically on credit.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=8)** Now, I know that you, Jim Stice, are an expert in these matters, so very roughly speaking, how many days elapsed from the time Nike buys raw materials until the time that Nike sells the finished shoes and sportswear?
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=21)** - Well, it's about 102 days.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=24)** We call that the number of days sales in inventory.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=28)** Okay, so how long until Nike collects cash from these sales to retail outlets?
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=33)** - On average, about 30 days.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=35)** We call that the average collection period.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=38)** If you take those two numbers together, 102 days from the purchase of the raw materials to the sale of the finished shoes and 30 days from the sale to the cash collection, you get 132 days, which is the length of Nike's operating cycle.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=53)** - Okay. Operating cycle?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=55)** - Yeah, that's the time from the purchase of the raw materials to the collection of the cash from the sale of finished goods for Nike, 132 days.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=64)** - Okay.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, I assume that Nike purchases its raw materials on standard credit terms from its suppliers.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=70)** So how long can Nike wait until it has to pay for those materials purchases?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=75)** - On average, Nike pays in about 38 days.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=78)** - Okay, so Nike buys raw materials on credit and it pays for those raw materials after about, you said 38 days?
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=86)** But Nike doesn't collect the cash from selling the finished shoes and sportswear till a total of 132 days have elapsed.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=93)** - Exactly.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=94)** This is why companies such as Nike must carefully manage purchases, sales, and cash collections in order to balance out this mismatch between the timing of cash payments and cash collections.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=107)** - [Instructor] Okay, so what about McDonald's?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=109)** - Well, I love the cash flow pattern of McDonald's.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=112)** From the time that McDonald's buys raw materials, that's food and packaging, until McDonald's sells the finished product, the food wrapped in the packaging, about six days elapse.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=123)** Cash collections takes about two seconds, because even when the customer uses a credit or debit card, McDonald's gets its electronic cash almost instantly.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=134)** So the length of McDonald's operating cycle is six days plus two seconds, six days from the purchase of the raw material until the sale, and then two more seconds to collect the cash.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=146)** - Now, that is rapid asset management.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-short-term-financial-management?u=76281980&t=149)** Let's talk about short term financial management.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - okay (3), - well (2), - on (2), - nike (1), - yeah (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)

#### The operating cycle
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=0)** - Remember, when we're talking about finance, we're talking about identifying the resources that we need, determining the best way to get the money to buy those resources, and then managing those resources effectively.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=13)** To do these things, we need detailed, timely information.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=18)** Let's now focus on short-term financial management.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=22)** We're going to illustrate this using the operating cycle of a wholesale building supply company.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=28)** You will notice in this diagram that we've got various points on the clock.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=32)** You'll note at two o'clock on the diagram we're purchasing inventory.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=36)** We typically purchase inventory on credit, so we have to have financial trust from our suppliers.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=43)** Also, from an operations standpoint, we've got to have suppliers that we can count on to get us what we need when we need it.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=52)** Once we've purchased that inventory, we've then got to manage our inventory.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=56)** The last thing we want is to not have enough inventory to satisfy our customers' demands.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=62)** The worst thing that can happen to a business is to run out of inventory.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=67)** When a contractor comes into our building supply business and needs sheet rock, we've got to have it.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=72)** We don't want to ever run out of inventory, but then we don't want to have too much inventory.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=77)** Where will we store it? How will we pay for it?
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=80)** We've got to manage relationships with our customers.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=83)** We have large customers. We'll have small customers.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=87)** We'll have customers we like, and we'll have customers who aren't our favorites.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=92)** We've got to ensure that we have a system to manage those relationships.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=97)** You'll note in the diagram that once we make the sale on credit, we end up with a receivable.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=103)** We've got to manage our receivables to ensure that we have prompt collections.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=108)** If we don't send our customers a bill, they're not just going to magically pay us.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=112)** We've got to manage our receivables to ensure that this operating cycle, this conversion from inventory to receivables to cash functions so that we eventually collect our cash in a timely fashion.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=126)** We're going to have to manage this operating cycle continuously, and we're going to need information to do that.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=134)** We also need to consider those instances when we may have a shortfall of cash.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=139)** How do we obtain short-term financing?
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=142)** Managing a company's operating cycle is like directing an orchestra.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=147)** Each supplier and each customer plays a part in the orchestra, and the manager needs to make sure that the inventory flows and the cash outflows and cash inflows all occur at the right time.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-operating-cycle?u=76281980&t=159)** That's operating cycle management.

#### Cash management
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about cash management.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=3)** First, why should a company have any cash at all?
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=5)** As we know, cash is a low-yielding asset.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=9)** The return on the cash I have in my checking account is less than 1%.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=14)** So a company doesn't want to have too much low-yielding cash, but a company also wants to have enough cash to handle routine needs.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=23)** Bills have to be paid in cash.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=25)** Employees have to be paid in cash.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=28)** Rent has to be paid in cash.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=29)** Insurance has to be paid in cash.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=32)** A company has to manage cash to ensure that the cash is there when it is needed.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=38)** If payroll's due to be paid on Friday and you don't have the cash, your entire operation will be thrown into chaos.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=45)** Cash is a low-yielding asset so you don't want to have too much, but you also want to avoid the chaos and cost associated with running out of cash.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cash-management?u=76281980&t=55)** In short, companies need to carefully manage their cash.
>
