#### Managing receivables and inventory
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=0)** - A company's operating cycle is the length of time from when the company buys its inventory until when the company collects the cash associated with selling that inventory.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=10)** The total length of the operating cycle is the sum of two time periods, the number of days that elapse from the purchases of the inventor to when it's sold, plus the number of days from the sale of the inventory until the customer pays in cash.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=26)** Walmart, for example, sells the inventory that it purchases from Toro, Procter & Gamble, and its other suppliers, in an average of 42 days.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=36)** This number is called the number of days sales in inventory.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=40)** That's an average across all Walmart product lines.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=43)** Some items sell faster than that and some take longer.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=46)** But on average, 42 days elapse from the time Walmart buys something from a supplier until Walmart sells that thing to you and me.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=56)** By comparison, the same computation for Target gives a number of days sales in inventory of 60 days.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=63)** Walmart runs its inventory through the system 18 days faster than does Target.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=69)** Proper inventory management involves balancing the desire to never run out of any inventory items, thus losing sales, with the need to keep the amount of money tied up in inventory to a low, reasonable amount.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=83)** Excess inventory just sitting on a shelf does not generate any profit, does not earn any interest, and does not pay any of a company's bills.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=92)** Until a company sells that inventory and then collects the selling price in cash, that company has to look for other sources of cash with which to pay its bills.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=103)** Now let's talk about the length of time from when a company sells its inventory to a customer until that customer pays in cash.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=111)** This is called the average collection period.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=114)** As we computed previously, the average collection period for Nike is 30 days.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=120)** That's an average across all Nike customers.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=123)** On average, 30 days elapsed from the time Nike sells something to its retail vendors, such as Foot Locker and Dick's Sporting Goods, until Nike collects the cash from that sale.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=135)** During the length of this average collection period, the company's cash is not in the company's pockets.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=142)** Instead, the company's cash is in the pockets of its customers.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=146)** Until a company collects that accounts receivable in cash, the company has to look for other sources of cash with which to pay its bills.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=155)** Now, those other sources of cash include the delay in payment of a company's obligations, delay in the purchase of necessary assets, and maybe even a costly short-term loan.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=167)** Nike's total operating cycle is 132 days.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=172)** 100 days from the purchase of raw materials until the sale of the finished goods, and then an additional 30 days to collect cash from the customers.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=181)** 132 days is almost four and a half months.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=185)** Nike has a long operating cycle.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=189)** Now, why is this number important to know?
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=192)** Because now I can start to manage it.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=195)** I can stay on top of my receivables, the companies that owe me money, to ensure that my customers pay in a timely fashion.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=202)** I can try to gently drive down the time I wait to collect the money owed me, and I can measure the results of my efforts by monitoring the average collection period number.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=214)** I can also work to manage my inventory better.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=217)** I can eliminate bottlenecks and streamline my production process to try and reduce the days that I hold inventory, and I can measure the results of my efforts by monitoring the number of days sales in inventory.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/managing-receivables-and-inventory?u=76281980&t=229)** Remember, a company's operating cycle is the length of time from when the company buys its inventory until when the company collects the cash associated with selling that inventory.

#### Obtaining short-term financing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=0)** - An important aspect of short-term financial management is obtaining short-term financing.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=6)** You remember the case of Toys"R"Us with the variable season cash flow pattern, low cash in October after a big inventory buildup, and then large cash balances in January after the holiday season sales and cash collections.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=21)** Because of this seasonal up and down fluctuation in cash, sometimes Toys"R"Us needs to borrow money in the short-term.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=29)** Well, what can toys us do to make sure that sufficient cash is always available?
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=34)** One tactic is simply to stretch out the time you take to pay your suppliers.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=38)** For example, by paying your suppliers in 60 days instead of 30 days, you might possibly be able to delay your payments until you start collecting cash from your customers.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=49)** In a similar fashion, you can strategically delay payments on other operating payables, such as to your advertising agency, your shipping company, or your maintenance service.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, of course, your inventory suppliers and other parties are not happy when you pay them more slowly.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=66)** So this use of operating payables as a source of short-term financing should be done very carefully and with advanced notice to your suppliers.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=76)** In addition to managing its short-term operating payables, sometimes a seasonal company such as Toys"R"Us needs to borrow money in the short-term.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=85)** One way to obtain this financing is just a straightforward short-term bank loan.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=90)** Go to the bank, ask for a loan, and promise the bank that you will repay the loan in a couple of months.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=97)** Another way to obtain short-term financing is to arrange all of this in advance by obtaining a line of credit.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=104)** You go to the bank with a cash needs forecast and you say, "My business is a seasonal one.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=110)** Over the next two years, I will be needing short-term cash from time to time.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=114)** Let's make the loan arrangements now."
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=118)** Now, if the bank judges your cash budget forecast to be acceptable, and if you can offer good collateral for the loans, such as granting the bank the right to claim your inventory, if you don't repay your loans and if the bank trusts your character, then you can be pre-approved for these periodic loans.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=135)** This is called the line of credit.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=138)** Many, many companies have pre-approved lines of credit.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=142)** For example, in the 2024 annual report of McDonald's, you can read about the company's $4 billion pre-approved line of credit.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=150)** This means that anytime McDonald's needs to borrow any amount up to $4 billion, the company's already approved for it.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=158)** Now, they pay a little fee for this line of credit, but the arrangement provides the company with financial flexibility and predictability.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=168)** Now, if you're a very credit-worthy company, a company such as General Motors or IBM, you can borrow money in the commercial paper market.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=176)** Commercial paper is just a fancy name for an IOU a piece of paper.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=181)** IBM writes something like the following on the IOU.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=185)** Please give us $100 million now and we promise to pay you back in 30 days or 60 days.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=191)** These IOUs are purchased by large investors or by financial institutions or dealers.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=197)** These commercial paper IOUs circulate in what is called the money market.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=201)** The interest rate on these short-term loans is lower than, for example, a five year loan.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=207)** As of January, 2025, the annual interest rate on a three month commercial paper loan for a highly rated company was just 4.33%.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=218)** Supplier financing lines of credit and commercial paper.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/obtaining-short-term-financing?u=76281980&t=223)** Obtaining short-term financing is a very important part of short-term financial management.

#### Internal controls
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=0)** - Another aspect of short-term financial management is internal controls.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, I admit this seems like a boring label.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=8)** Internal controls, something only accountants would care about, but internal control is the label given to the important systems in place inside a company to make sure that the company does what it is supposed to do, that it satisfies its obligations, and that everything gets recorded.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=27)** Turns out, if you don't have a data collection system, if you don't have a paper trail, then you're not able to manage your processes at all.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=35)** You may have heard of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=39)** This company regulation was passed in the United States in 2002 in the wake of several large financial and accounting scandals.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=47)** A key provision of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act is that each publicly traded US company must have a system of internal controls in place, and the existence of these controls has to be verified by an external auditor.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=62)** To repeat, internal controls make sure that companies do what they're supposed to do, and that everything gets recorded.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=69)** Let me give you some examples of good internal control practices.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=74)** One example is the separation of duties.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=76)** Think of separation of duties like this.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=79)** Hands that touch assets should not also be allowed to touch the records where we keep track of those assets.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=87)** Imagine a warehouse in a manufacturing facility where you keep the raw materials.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=93)** If a person has access to the raw materials and also has access to the records where we keep track of which raw materials are there, the person could steal raw materials and cover that up by doctoring the books.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=107)** Go to a supermarket.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=109)** You'll see that the cashiers who have access to assets, cash, do not also have access to the records where we keep track of that cash.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=118)** That record-keeping system is embedded in the cash register.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=123)** The cash register is a perfect example of an internal control, separation of duties.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=129)** Hands that touch assets should not also be allowed to touch the records where we keep track of those assets.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=136)** Another example of an internal control practice is the physical protection of the assets.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=142)** Cash gets locked away.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=143)** Computers are password-protected.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=146)** A few years ago, I heard an executive of a high-tech company in the United States talk about the importance of internal controls.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=154)** This person said that one of the most important assets of this high-tech company is their intellectual property.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=160)** So, I asked, how do you protect this intellectual property when you're traveling?
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=165)** Presumably, your personal computer holds corporate secrets.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=169)** This corporate executive replied, "Our intellectual property is so important that when I travel, company policy does not allow me to take my laptop with me.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=179)** I'm given a special laptop containing my presentation files and nothing else."
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=186)** This restrictive laptop policy is an example of an internal control in place to protect company assets, intellectual property in this case.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=195)** Now, another aspect of internal control is independent checks.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=200)** Employees never know when an auditor will come, either an internal auditor from inside a company, or an external auditor from outside the company.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=209)** Another internal control is that companies sometimes have a system in place requiring employees to take regular vacations.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=217)** This control exists because if an employee is committing fraud or theft, the employee can cover it up because he or she is always there to smooth out any questions that might arise.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/internal-controls?u=76281980&t=228)** Internal controls protect the assets, make sure the records are complete, and also make sure that a company satisfies its obligations.

### 5. Obtaining Long-Term Financing

#### Introducing long-term financing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=0)** - US census data revealed that the median selling price for the new homes sold in the United States in the month October, 2024 was $425,300.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=12)** - One of the key questions in corporate finance is whether you can impact the value of an asset, such as a house or an entire company, by strategically choosing the mix of borrowing and owner investment used to finance the purchase of the asset.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=25)** This topic area is called capital structure.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=28)** - Let's illustrate this topic of capital structure using a hypothetical house costing 425,000.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=36)** Assume that we have two ways to finance the purchase of the house.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=39)** - [Instructor] Number one, use personal savings to pay cash for the entire amount.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=44)** So you have no mortgage loan on the house at all.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=46)** You own the house free and clear.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=48)** - [Instructor] Number two, use personal savings to pay for a small amount of the purchase price, say 42,500 or 10%, and take out a large mortgage loan for the remaining 90% of $382,500.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=63)** - Okay, let's think about your personal experience with this house, and whether that experience differs depending on how you finance the purchase of the house.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=70)** Number one, you own it free and clear.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=74)** And number two, you have a large mortgage loan of $382,500 on the house.
>

>

>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=87)** Are these things impacted by whether you have a mortgage loan on the house?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=92)** No. - How about the quality of the local schools, the niceness of your neighbors across the street?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=97)** Are these things impacted by whether you had a mortgage loan on the house?
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=101)** No. No, not at all.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=102)** - So really, the house, as a house, as a place to live is not impacted by whether you own the house free and clear compared to having a large mortgage loan on the house.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=113)** Are there any differences?
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=115)** - (chuckles) Well, absolutely.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=116)** The probability that financial distress could cause you to lose the house is much greater if you have a mortgage loan.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=123)** If you can't make your mortgage payments, eventually you're going to lose the house.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=128)** If you own the house free and clear, you don't have to worry about making payments.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=132)** So this suggests that you're better off to have a very small mortgage on your house.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=136)** - On the other hand, at least in the United States, any interest you pay on your mortgage loan gives you an income tax deduction.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=144)** Each year you will pay less income tax to the government if you have a large mortgage loan.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=150)** So this suggests that you are better off to have a very large mortgage on your house.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=155)** - So those are the conflicting forces.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=158)** A small mortgage reduces the risk of losing your house because of financial distress, but a large mortgage reduces the amount of income taxes you pay to the government.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=166)** Same house, but the total value of the house depends on how you finance the purchase.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=172)** - Now, when applied to the financing of purchasing of the assets for an entire company, this is the issue of capital structure.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-long-term-financing?u=76281980&t=180)** Turns out it's one of the most important in the fields of corporate finance.

#### Does capital structure matter? No?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980)

> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=113)** That's the capital structure question.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=116)** Does capital structure impact the value of the company?
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=120)** Now in the area of corporate finance, this is the question, does capital structure impact value?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=127)** To talk about this, we need to do a little historical discussion.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=132)** The first sophisticated analysis of this question was done in 1958 by Professors Modigliani and Miller.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=140)** They said, no, they're finding no, doesn't make any difference whether you borrow the money or whether the money is owner invested.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=148)** The financing choice does not impact the value of the company, so don't worry about capital structure.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=155)** Okay, now, to derive this result, Professors Modigliani and Miller made some extreme assumptions.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=163)** In their mathematical model, they assumed that there are no taxes, no bankruptcy costs, no differences in information among people, and no frictions that hinder business In a perfect frictionless Modigliani-Miller world, it doesn't matter whether the company borrows the money for financing or the investors invest money from their personal savings, or the owners borrow the money themselves and then invest it in the company.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=191)** In the perfect Modigliani-Miller world, the financing choice doesn't have any impact on the value of the company.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=198)** This is the baseline capital structure result, the Modigliani and Miller Theorem.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=205)** Now these two professors won Nobel Prizes for this powerful result.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=209)** So the natural question is this, what happens if there are frictions such as taxes and bankruptcy costs?
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-no?u=76281980&t=218)** Well, keep watching.

#### Does capital structure matter? Yes?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the perfect Modigliani-Miller world, the financing choice doesn't have any impact on the value of the company.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=8)** But this result is derived in a sterile world where there are no frictions, no taxes, no transactions costs, and no differences in information among people.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=19)** Let's think about the real world.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=21)** There are frictions.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=22)** There are transactions costs.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=24)** There are income taxes.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=26)** The analytical work done after Modigliani and Miller loosened these assumptions and found that capital structure does impact the value of a company when there are costs and frictions, with income taxes being a key factor.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=41)** You see, if I borrow money, then any payments I make to the people who provided the capital to the company, who loaned money to the company, that's called interest expense.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, this is important because interest expense is reported on my income tax form as a tax deduction, so I pay less in taxes.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=61)** If those same providers of capital had given me the money as an investment, then any money I paid back to them would be called dividends.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=70)** And in most countries, businesses don't get a tax deduction for paying dividends.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=75)** So if the providers of capital are called lenders, then the payments to them, interest, are tax deductible.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=83)** If the providers of capital are called investors, then the payments to them, dividends, are not tax deductible.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=90)** Classifying amounts received from providers of capital as loans lowers the amount of income tax that the company pays.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=98)** Of course, this suggests that there should never be any owner investment.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=102)** Whenever a company wants to get money, the company should borrow the money because then all the payments to providers of capital are tax-deductible interest and the company will pay a lower amount of income taxes.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=114)** So those are the two extremes.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=117)** In the perfect Modigliani-Miller world, it doesn't make any difference to the value of the company whether capital comes from loans or owner investment.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=126)** But in a world with income taxes and tax-deductible interest payments, companies should borrow all the money they need for financing.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=136)** As with most things in life, the real answer lies somewhere in between these theoretical extremes.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=144)** There are forces pushing both ways.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=146)** For example, does a home mortgage impact the value of your house?
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=152)** A high mortgage means you get a very high tax deduction, so that would suggest you should have a high mortgage, but a high mortgage puts you at risk of losing your house if you can't make the mortgage payments.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=164)** So you probably want a lower mortgage.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=167)** With respect to a company's capital structure, we've already talked about the income tax benefit to borrowing, but there are additional benefits to borrowing.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=176)** One benefit is that a company with borrowing must be run efficiently and without wasted money in order to be able to make the mandatory loan and interest payments.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=187)** When the labor union comes to that company and says, "Listen, we want higher wages," this high leverage company can respond, "Well, we'd like to give you higher wages but we can't because we have to make sure that we can make all these interest payments."
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=202)** Companies with higher borrowing have been shown to have less employee slack.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=206)** Things are run more tightly.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=208)** Now, that's maybe not a pleasant situation to be in as an employee, but it does increase the value of the company from the standpoint of the owners.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=218)** But too much leverage increases the probability of bankruptcy.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=222)** A company going through bankruptcy proceedings is in a very costly situation in terms of legal fees.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=230)** In addition, in a company close to bankruptcy, company management is distracted.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=235)** You're not thinking of long-term marketing plans when you're going through bankruptcy proceedings.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=241)** Now, is there an optimal capital structure?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=245)** Yes, there is.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-yes?u=76281980&t=246)** It should have some borrowing because of the tax benefits, but not too much because of the increase in the probability of costly financial distress.

#### Does capital structure matter? Factors influencing optimal capital structure
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=0)** - So what is the optimal capital structure?
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=4)** Well, it depends on the specific business circumstances.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=8)** For example, let's do a little thought experiment.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=11)** If income tax rates are high, should a company have more borrowing in its capital structure or less?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=18)** Let's think this through.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=19)** If tax rates are high, that means I get a bigger tax deduction for interest.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=24)** If I get a bigger tax deduction for interest, then loans are relatively cheap, and I want to borrow relatively more.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=32)** So in a situation where income tax rates are high, companies would tend to have more borrowing in their capital structure.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=40)** What about the quality of the collateral associated with the loans?
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=44)** If a company has very good collateral, then it is more likely to borrow more.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=48)** For example, if I'm borrowing the money to pay the wages of employees, well, that's not very good collateral, but if I'm borrowing the money to build a building, that's great collateral.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=59)** Banks are more willing to loan based on good collateral.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=63)** And what about the stability of the cash flows of the business?
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=66)** If you have a company that has very stable, predictable cash flows, you're able to borrow more money.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=72)** Stable cash flows give comfort to the lender so you can borrow more.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=76)** With volatile cash flows, banks are probably going to insist that you borrow relatively less.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=82)** Let's look at two examples, two of your favorite companies and mine, Walmart and Google, they have very different capital structures.
>

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=97)** Well, they'll use it to buy land, buildings, trucks, and other tangible things.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=102)** Now, let's imagine a catastrophic scenario in which something goes wrong with Walmart's business model and they can't pay us back, we're the bank, in this case.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=111)** Well all is not lost because the law is with us.

>

>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=193)** As a result, Google has received most of its long-term financing from investor investment, 70%, meaning that only 30% has been borrowed.

> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=203)** Note, Google's choice of low leverage, of course, is much more complex than is portrayed in this simple discussion.
>

>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/does-capital-structure-matter-factors-influencing-optimal-capital-structure?u=76281980&t=226)** Yes, there is, and that level of leverage will differ depending on the circumstances of the individual company.

#### Cost of capital: All debt or all equity financing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=0)** - The issue of capital structure is important because it impacts the cost of a company's capital.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=6)** Well, what is the cost of capital, and what difference does it make?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=11)** Cost of capital is the cost of obtaining external financing, which is a combination of the cost of borrowing and the cost of equity investment.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=20)** The cost of capital is critical because it determines which projects are going to be profitable and which projects are not, which projects are going to be started and which are not.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=31)** The higher cost of capital indicates an increased risk associated with the firm's operations, and increased risk means a higher required return by investors.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=43)** Now, the cost of borrowing, one source of capital, is just the interest rate on the loans.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=49)** The cost of equity investment, the other source of capital, is the return that investors need to expect to encourage them to invest in a project.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=57)** This cost of equity investment is what we compute using the capital asset pricing model.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=63)** So why does this cost of capital matter?
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=66)** Let's look at an example.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=68)** Let's say that my cost of capital is 11%, and I'm looking at a project that will generate returns of 15%.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=75)** That's beautiful.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=76)** I get my external financing at a cost of just 11%, and I use that to launch a project that is going to generate returns of 15%.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=85)** I've generated a surplus of 4%.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=87)** That's good. That's a good project.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=90)** On the other hand, if my cost of capital is 11% and it looks like a project is only going to generate returns of 7%, that's a horrible project.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=100)** So the cost of capital determines for a company which projects are good and which projects are bad, which projects we fund and which we don't.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=111)** Let's illustrate the importance of the cost of capital with some numbers with our ice cream company example.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=117)** Remember, Lily needs $200 million to pursue her dreams.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=121)** Case one, simple capital structure, 100% debt financing.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=127)** Lily borrows the entire $200 million.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=131)** Potential lenders look at this and demand an interest rate of 12% on these loans.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=136)** In other words, the lenders give you $200 million now, and at the end of one year, you need to give them back $200 million plus 12%.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=146)** That's $224 million.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=149)** Now let's look at the projected cash flow that will be generated by this ice cream business that creates lots of strawberry ice cream.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=157)** The cash flows are only going to be $220 million.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=161)** From the standpoint of the lenders, if this project is only going to generate $220 million in cash flow, it's no good.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=168)** It will not generate enough cash flow to repay the lenders for the risk they bear in loaning the money.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=175)** $220 million is less than $224 million.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=179)** The cost of capital is higher than the returns that are going to be generated by the project.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=184)** So this capital structure doesn't work.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=187)** All right, let's try something else.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=189)** Case two, 100% equity financing.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=192)** Lily finds investors who will provide the $200 million in an investment, not a loan.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=199)** These investors have used the capital asset pricing model, and they are demanding an expected return of 15%.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=206)** In other words, at the end of one year, there has to be $230 million available to give to the investors.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=213)** Now, why is this 15% equity financing return higher than the 12% interest rate in the 100% debt financing scenario?
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=222)** It's because debt financing is supported, protected by the laws of every country in the world.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=229)** If you loan money to a company, the law's on your side if the company doesn't pay you back.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=234)** If Lily can't pay back a $200 million loan, the lenders go to the courts, and the courts will allow the lenders to go in and take Lily's assets and do whatever they can to get their money back.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=245)** The law is on the side of the lenders.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=249)** Investors, on the other hand, are at greater risk.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=252)** If the investors can't get back their investment, the courts won't help them.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=256)** Owners accept the risk of a business.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=258)** It is riskier to invest in a company than it is to loan money to a company.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=264)** That's why this 15% equity investment return is higher than the 12% for the lending.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=270)** Now, in this case, in either case, the cost of capital is too high, so this project is not going to happen.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-all-debt-or-all-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=276)** The high cost of capital is preventing Lily's dream of an ice cream company from becoming a reality.

#### Cost of capital: Split debt-equity financing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=0)** - When it comes to the financing of a company's operations, rarely is it all or nothing.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=6)** In most cases, owners have put money in and there's also some borrowing as well.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=11)** So let's understand the trade off between debt and equity financing and why the proper mix can make all the difference.
>

>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=21)** Lily needs $200 million to finance her project, and the project is expected to generate cash flows of $220 million.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=29)** Let's now analyze a mix of two financing options.
>

>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=42)** The investors insist on a rate of return of 16%.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=46)** The lenders accept a rate of return of just 5%.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=50)** In this case, the total cost of capital is a $100 million plus 5%, or $105 million, for the lenders, and $100 million plus 16%, or 116 million, for the investors.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=63)** The total cost of capital, $221 million.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=67)** Now, first of all, why is this 16% required rate of return by the investors in this case higher than the 15% we already considered?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=77)** It's because these investors are in a riskier position because they are now paired with lenders.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=84)** Remember, we know that the courts favor lenders.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=87)** Let's say that this project generates only $150 million during the year.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=91)** People losing money here.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=93)** Who gets to collect cash first?

> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=96)** The lenders do.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=98)** With $150 million available, the lenders get to go in and take their entire $105 million first.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=106)** The investors get whatever's left over.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=108)** They're called the residual claimants.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=110)** They investors in this case are in a riskier position because of the existence of the priority claimants, the lenders.
>

>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=133)** The forecast is that the cash flow will be $220 million.
>

>

>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=140)** The cost of capital determines whether a project is going to occur or not.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=146)** Alright, let's try one more case. Case four.

>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cost-of-capital-split-debt-equity-financing?u=76281980&t=185)** From the standpoint of the investors who are providing 10% of the equity, remember, in the eyes of the law, the lenders collect first.
>

>

#### Weighted-average cost of capital
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=0)** - We are now ready to talk about the important topic of the weighted average cost of capital, or the WACC.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=8)** As we have seen, a company's capital structure determines how costly it is to obtain external financing.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=14)** Recall the example where Lily gets half of her financing from lenders, and half of her financing from investors.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=21)** The weighted average cost of capital is then just the average of the cost of those two sources of financing, 5% for the lenders, 16% for the investors, the weighted average cost of capital is computed by just putting those two together with a half weight on each of them, the average is 10.5%, that's the WACC.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=42)** How is Lily going to use this 10.5% WACC number?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=46)** Well, she will compare this 10.5% cost of capital to the 10% operating return she's expecting to generate from her ice cream business, because the 10.5% weighted average cost of capital, or WACC, is greater than the 10% return from the project, that is not a good project.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=66)** On the other hand, if the project were expected to generate a return of 15%, with a WACC of only 10.5%, this would be a very good project.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=75)** The weighted average cost of capital tells a company this, how much does it cost us to get our money?
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=82)** And then the company uses that WACC number to evaluate the attractiveness of various projects.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=88)** Consider the case where the financing was 90% debt and 10% equity.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=93)** Recall in this instance, that lenders required an 8% return, and investors required an 18% return.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=101)** The weighted average cost of capital would be this.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=104)** The WACC is equal to 8% X 0.9, weight 90% of the financing, + 18% X a 10% weight.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=114)** That all equals a WACC of 9%.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=117)** The weighted average cost of capital is computed as the cost of the debt multiplied by the fraction of the financing provided by the lenders, plus the cost of equity, multiplied by the proportion of the financing provided by the investors.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=132)** In this case, the 90/10 financing split, the weighted average cost of capital is 9%.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=139)** You can think of the whole purpose of capital structure and finding the optimal capital structure as being to find the capital structure that will give a company its lowest WACC, or weighted average cost of capital, lowest WACC.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=154)** If your cost of capital is lower, that means there are a greater number of attractive projects out there.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=160)** If my cost of capital is just 9%, I can find many more attractive projects than if my cost of capital is 15%.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/weighted-average-cost-of-capital?u=76281980&t=168)** The objective of corporate finance and capital structure is to find the capital structure that will provide the lowest weighted average cost of capital, the WACC.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** wacc (11)
> **Versions:** 10.5 (5), 0.9 (1)
> **Code Keywords:** this, (1), return, (1), return. (1), this. (1), case, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (3)
> **Speakers:** - we (1)


### 6. Understanding Securities Markets

#### Introducing securities markets
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=0)** - A securities market, like a stock exchange, bond exchange, or money market, is essentially a place either physical or virtual where people can come together and trade financial securities.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=12)** Stocks are a type of financial security that grant shareholders ownership rights in a company.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=18)** Bonds, on the other hand, are financial securities that represent a long-term loan arrangement where a company is the borrower and the bond holder is the lender.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=27)** Now, commercial paper refers to an IOU, a document that circulates in the money market, symbolizing a short-term loan between a large company and a money market investor.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=39)** A good example of a securities market is the New York Stock Exchange, which started way back in 1792 under a buttonwood tree in New York's Wall Street.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=49)** This is where people who own stocks or those representing them could meet and buy or sell those stocks.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=56)** - [Jim] Now, a virtual example would be the nasdaq, which began in 1971.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=61)** It started as a computer system where people could post messages like, I want to sell my stocks, or I want to buy stocks.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=68)** Serving as a virtual meeting space for traders.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=72)** - Do you, Jim Stice, consider yourself to be quite knowledgeable in the areas of accounting and finance?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=78)** - I do know a thing or two. - Okay.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=82)** So using your great expertise, could you pick winning investments in securities markets like the New York Stock Exchange?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=87)** - Well, not really. Markets like those are just too fast.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=90)** They respond to new information in a matter of minutes.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=94)** - By the time you have used your analysis to pick an undervalued stock, it's a week, two weeks, or two months, too late.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=102)** - Okay, got it.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=102)** So who determines the price of financial securities in the market?
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=106)** Well, it turns out that no one sets the price in the market.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=110)** Instead, the price arises from the interactions of the buyers and sellers in the market.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=115)** The human interactions in the market aggregate all information available until both the sellers and the buyers agree that the price fairly reflects that information.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=125)** - The agreed upon price then is the market price at that moment.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=129)** - Okay so what causes security prices to change?
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=132)** Well, it's new information.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=134)** - Yep, and when new information becomes available, potential buyers and potential sellers incorporate into the price very quickly in well established markets.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=143)** If the news is good, price goes up.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=146)** If news is bad, the price goes down.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-securities-markets?u=76281980&t=149)** - Through the buying and selling that takes place in securities markets prices adjust to reflect new information about the factors that influence future cash flows that come from owning stocks, bonds, commercial paper, and other financial securities.
