> **Definitions:** refers to (1)

#### The difference between a stock and a bond
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about two basic types of financial securities: stocks and bonds.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=6)** A stock is evidence of ownership in a company, and a bond is evidence that the bondholder has loaned money to the company.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=14)** To illustrate the difference between the two, consider this.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=18)** From outer space, you are watching, through a telescope, a transaction between an individual and a company.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=25)** You see the individual give the company money.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=27)** You see the company give the individual a piece of paper.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=31)** Did the company sell a stock or a bond?
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=34)** From space, that transaction looks the same, exchanging money for a piece of paper.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=40)** Now, although the issuance of stocks and bonds looks the same from a distance, they represent fundamentally different transactions.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=49)** A stock is evidence that a stockholder is an owner of a portion or a share of a corporation.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=56)** If you own a share of stock, you're an owner in that corporation.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=60)** The stockholder receives the stock typically in exchange for paying cash to the previous owner of the share.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=67)** A bond, on the other hand, is evidence that the bondholder is owed money by the company that issued the bond.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=74)** In essence, a bond is an IOU.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=77)** The bondholder receives the bond typically in exchange for paying cash to the previous owner of the bond.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=84)** A share of stock is evidence of ownership.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=87)** A bond is evidence of a loan.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=90)** Bonds are often called debt. Stocks are often called equity.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=95)** Now let's look at the difference between pure debt and pure equity.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=99)** We're going to look at two extremes, just pure debt and pure equity to highlight the difference between the two.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=105)** For example, do annual payments have to be made?
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=108)** Well, with pure debt, interest payments are mandatory.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=113)** Whether the company does well or the company does poorly, interest on debt has to be paid.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=120)** On the other hand, with equity, with stock, do payments have to be made?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=125)** Those payments are called dividends.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=127)** They don't have to be made.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=128)** The company can choose to pay a dividend or not.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=132)** With debt, interest payments have to be made.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=135)** With dividends, the payments are optional.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=137)** Now, what about the amount of the payment?
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=140)** Well, typically with pure debt, the amount of the interest payment is fixed.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=145)** The person who invested in those bonds can expect a regular fixed interest payment on those bonds.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=151)** On the other hand, with pure equity, the dividend amount is not fixed.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=155)** It can go up or it can go down.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=158)** Okay, another, does the initial investment have to be repaid?
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=162)** Well, with debt, a loan, the amount has to be repaid in the future.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=166)** There are maturity dates associated with debt, and a specific amount has to be repaid.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=172)** With equity, on the other hand, there's no requirement that any investment amount be repaid.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=178)** With stock, with equity, you're an owner.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=180)** Your company, the corporation, does not legally have to pay you back the amount of your initial investment.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=187)** In other words, your investment amount is at risk.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=191)** With debt, the company is obligated to pay you back.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=194)** With equity, there's no obligation.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=197)** One more difference between stocks and bonds is the order of priority in case of bankruptcy.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=203)** Who first gets to extract any remaining assets?
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=206)** Well, debt holders collect first and equity holders get what's left over, if anything's left over.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-difference-between-a-stock-and-a-bond?u=76281980&t=213)** There are differences between stocks and bonds in terms of whether regular payments are mandatory, whether the amount of those payments is fixed or variable, whether the initial investment amount has to be repaid, and who collects first in case of bankruptcy.

#### Stock markets
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we know what a stock is and what a bond is, let's talk about stock markets and bond markets.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=7)** Now, here's some unsettling questions to consider.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=11)** Are stock markets just places for people to gamble?

> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=30)** After five years, this car can be sold as a used car for $15,000.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=36)** So let's think about that original transaction.

>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=48)** The car dealer does.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=50)** That is a simple transaction.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=52)** That's a new car transaction.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=54)** Five years later, when you sell the used car for $15,000, who pays the 15,000?
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=60)** The person who buys the used car from you.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=63)** Who receives the 15,000?
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=65)** Well, you do.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=66)** You're selling your used car.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=68)** And who doesn't get anything from that used car sale five years later?
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=72)** The $15,000?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=74)** Well, the original car dealership doesn't get anything of that $15,000.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=79)** There is no direct connection in cash flow terms between the used car market and that new car dealer.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=86)** But does the existence of this used car market impact me when I'm originally thinking of buying the new car?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=94)** Do I care that there is a used car market out there?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=97)** Yeah, I do because I know if I buy the car now, if I get tired of this car after a year or two or five, I'll be able to sell it to somebody else.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=106)** The existence of the used car market makes it more likely that I'll buy the new car because I can get rid of it at some time in the future if I want to.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=116)** This helps the new car dealer sell new cars because buyers are more willing to buy new cars knowing that they won't be stuck with them forever.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=126)** Now you can think of a stock market as just a place to sell you shares of stock.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=132)** The shares were originally issued to shareholders, and now those shares are trading on the used share market.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=140)** Just as with new cars and used cars, the existence of a stock market makes shareholders more willing to invest and buy shares in the first place.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=151)** Shareholders think, okay, I'll invest in this company because if I decide I don't want to be an owner in this company anymore and I want to take my money elsewhere, I can sell my shares in the used share market.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=163)** This is part of the social value of a stock market.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/stock-markets?u=76281980&t=166)** The existence of the stock market makes it more likely that people will invest in stocks in the first place.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (4), else. (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Speakers:** - now (1)

#### Bond markets
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are many different kinds of bonds, and most bond trades are arranged by brokers who connect individual sellers and buyers of bonds.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=10)** There are government bonds that are issued by countries.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=13)** These are sometimes called sovereign bonds.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=16)** The United States government has issued many, many bonds.
>

>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=29)** These municipal bonds are ways for local governments to borrow money.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=33)** There are corporate bonds which are issued by companies.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=37)** About 30% of the bonds outstanding in the world are issued by companies.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=42)** Now, some bonds are called asset-backed bonds.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=45)** These are quite interesting things.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=47)** These asset-backed bonds are backed by a set of assets, maybe a company's accounts receivables, or a set of mortgages on residential homes.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=57)** Somebody has gathered ownership of these things together, these mortgages or these accounts receivable, and then they make some bonds and say, "These bonds will be repaid "with the proceeds from these accounts receivable "or from the proceeds of these mortgages."
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=73)** This process of bundling the cash flows from these assets is called securitization.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=79)** And this all sounds very nice, straightforward, and safe.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=83)** But asset-backed bonds, securitization of mortgage loans payable, for example, helped to bring down the entire worldwide economy in 2008 when the assets that were backing those bonds proved not to be as strong as they had originally been expected to be.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, not all corporate borrowing, by the way, is through bonds.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=105)** Corporate bonds issued by a company are referred to as being the company's public debt.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=111)** Most corporate borrowing happens privately through private arrangements, such as borrowing directly from banks or by a consortium of banks.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=119)** This is called the company's private debt.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=123)** So with respect to corporate borrowing, part of it is called public debt.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=127)** That's the corporate bonds.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=129)** Part of it is called private debt, which is composed of individual loans that have been arranged directly with banks or a set of banks.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=137)** Now, are there bond markets out there?

> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=140)** Remember, bonds are just pieces of paper and shares of stock are just pieces of paper.

> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=145)** So if there are stock markets, are there also bond markets?

>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=160)** Somebody's got a bond and they want to sell it.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=162)** So they call a dealer or a broker, or an investment bank who finds a buyer and the trade happens directly.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=170)** This is called over-the-counter trading.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=173)** Why are bond trades done over-the-counter?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=176)** Well, with stocks, there are only in the United States about 4,000 publicly traded stocks, and all stocks of a certain company are the same.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=185)** And so a share of stock in a given company is a homogeneous commodity.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=190)** It's easy to trade stocks.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=192)** With bonds, on the other hand, there are tens of thousands of companies that have issued bonds in the United States, and those bonds have different characteristics.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=202)** So when I say I want a bond of AT&T, well, which AT&T bond?

> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=207)** There are many, many different bonds of AT&T or many, many different bonds of General Motors, all with different characteristics such as interest rates, maturity date, and collateral privileges.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=219)** So there are lots of different kinds of bonds, even for the same company.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=224)** The market for bonds is just a more complex market.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=228)** Now, for most bonds, the trading is said to be thin, meaning that days could go by and there could be no trades for that bond.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=236)** Even the most infrequently traded stock trades pretty much every single day.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=241)** But sometimes bonds don't trade for days or weeks on end.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/bond-markets?u=76281980&t=246)** So bond market trading is done more over-the-counter, where the trades are individually arranged by a broker who puts together the seller of the bond and the buyer of the bond.

#### Publicly-traded shares: What impacts the share price?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=0)** - So who sets the prices of the shares of stock trading in a stock market, and what factors influence those prices?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=8)** Well, consider these related questions: Who sets the price of a gallon of milk?
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=13)** Who sets the price of a gallon of gasoline?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=16)** Who sets the selling price of a house?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=18)** The answer to all of these questions is that no one sets the price in a market.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=24)** Instead, the price arises from the interactions of the buyers and sellers in the market.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=30)** If the price is too low, buyers will flood the market.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=33)** Sellers will quickly sense this and will insist on a higher price.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=38)** If the price is too high, sellers will flood the market and buyers will be able to be choosy.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=43)** They won't buy unless prices are lowered.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=46)** The human interactions in the market aggregate all information available until both the sellers and the buyers agree that the price fairly reflects that information.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=57)** So that is how the prices of milk, gasoline, houses, children's clothing, and every other price is determined in a freely functioning market.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=66)** In the case of prices of shares of stock, the process is exactly the same.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=72)** When both the seller and the buyer consider the share price to be fair given the available information, they trade.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=80)** That trading price is then considered to be the market share price at the moment.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=86)** So what causes share prices to change?
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=90)** New information.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=92)** New information about future sales and net income of the company.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=96)** New information about overall economic trends, new information about industry-related events, and new information about political stability and investor confidence.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=108)** All of this new information is considered by potential buyers and sellers, and is then quickly impounded into the share price, causing the price to go up if the news is good and down if the news is bad.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=122)** This process of assimilating new information happens pretty fast in active markets.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=128)** For example, for companies traded on the New York Stock Exchange or the Nasdaq, any information is reflected in prices in just a few minutes.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=138)** Now, why would a company want to allow its ownership shares to be traded in a public market?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=144)** This process of listing your shares in a public stock exchange is called going public, and the event itself is called an initial public offering, or IPO.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=156)** One benefit of being publicly listed is that it's easier to raise capital when you're public.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=162)** You can issue shares in exchange for cash to investors all over the world.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=168)** In addition, if your shares are traded publicly, there is a liquid market for shareholders to sell their shares.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=176)** For example, employees who have received shares as part of their compensation can easily exchange those shares for cash.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=184)** Another benefit of being publicly traded is that there is a boost to a company's reputation when it has decided to expose itself to constant public scrutiny.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=195)** In order to survive that scrutiny, a publicly traded company is probably better than average.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=202)** There are benefits associated with going public, but there are costs as well.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=207)** If your shares are publicly traded, you're subject to increased regulation.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=212)** There are government agencies who are following your company.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=215)** There are financial reports you have to submit that then become public information.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=220)** If you are publicly traded, there are a lot of impatient people watching you.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=225)** You may no longer be able to take the long-term view.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=229)** Your planning horizon might not even stretch beyond next quarter's earnings report.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=234)** Another cost of going public is that the founders can lose control of the company.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=239)** The new shareholders may have different ideas about how the company should be run.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=244)** So there are both benefits and costs associated with being a publicly traded company.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=250)** The vast majority of companies in the world, they're not publicly traded.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/publicly-traded-shares-what-impacts-the-share-price?u=76281980&t=254)** For example, in the United States, there are about 4,000 companies that are publicly traded and about 15 million companies that are not.

#### Initial public offering (IPO): Microsoft
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=0)** - When a company offers its shares to the public for the first time, the event is called an IPO, or Initial Public Offering.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=9)** Because there's no active buying and selling before the shares are listed publicly, an initial price must be set just to get things going.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=18)** This initial price is set by the listing company and its financial advisors.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=24)** Let's take a look at an example of a company that went public almost 40 years ago.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=28)** Microsoft went public on March 13th of 1986.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=33)** The company had been operating since 1975 as a privately-held company.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=39)** A number of employees had been given shares as part of their compensation.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=43)** But there was no market in which to trade those shares.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=47)** In 1985, the year before the IPO, Microsoft had earnings per share of $1.10.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=54)** Now at the time of Microsoft's IPO, there were several other software companies that were already publicly traded.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=62)** Those companies on average were trading at a P/E ratio, or price-to-earnings ratio, of about 15.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=70)** In other words, the market value of those companies were about 15 times as large as those companies most recent annual net income.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=79)** So what is a reasonable estimate of the initial value of Microsoft's shares given that the company's most recent earnings per share was $1.10?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=87)** Well, that initial estimate which founder Bill Gates and his financial advisors used, was $16.50 per share.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=95)** Computed as $1.10 earnings per share multiplied by the industry average P/E ratio of 15.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=103)** Okay, this is an initial rough estimate.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=106)** But would investors buy the shares at that $16.50 price?
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=111)** Bill Gates and his financial advisors did what companies always do as they're preparing to go public.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=116)** They conducted a road show.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=118)** They went around the world and gave presentations about Microsoft to large investors, mutual funds, pension funds, and so forth.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=127)** They told these large investors that the initial price per share estimate was $16.50.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=133)** And asked them if they would be willing to invest at that price.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=137)** At that $16.50 price, lots of people wanted to buy Microsoft shares.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=143)** Now, who suffers if the initial share price is too low?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=147)** Well, the initial shareholders, such as the founders Bill Gates and his partner Paul Allen, are not fully-compensated for their work in creating the company.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=157)** Well, what is the danger if the initial price is too high?
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=160)** Well then, nobody wants to buy those over-priced shares.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=163)** The IPO shares are said to be undersubscribed.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=166)** This results in bad publicity.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=168)** So what happened?
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=171)** Given the positive feedback from the road show, Bill Gates and his investment advisors decided to set the initial share price at $21 a share.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=180)** A bit of an increase over the initial $16.50 number computed using the P/E ratio.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=187)** The Microsoft shares proved to be an instant success in the market.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=191)** At the end of the first day of trading, the share price was up to $27.75.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=197)** About a 30% increase in one day.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=201)** Contrast this with Uber's IPO.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=204)** Uber went public at $45 a share on May 10th, 2019.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=210)** Their stock dropped on day one and one month later they were selling at around $41 per share.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=216)** About a 10% drop for the month. Hey, not all IPOs win.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=222)** The IPO price is set using accounting data, industry data, and the investor interest uncovered during the road show.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=231)** Once the shares are trading in the market, the interactions of buyers and sellers determine the price.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=236)** So back to Microsoft.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=238)** What is the current price of those Microsoft shares that sold for $21 in the IPO?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=245)** Well those shares have been multiplied in a process called stock splits.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=249)** So each original share is now equaled to 288 shares.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=254)** As of January 30th, 2025, the aggregate price of those shares is about $119,557.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/initial-public-offering-ipo-microsoft?u=76281980&t=264)** From the IPO price of $21, up to $120,000. That's not bad.

### 7. Financial Institutions

#### Introducing financial institutions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, let's talk about grandchildren.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=3)** - Boy, this is where you tell 'em how you have about 1000 grandchildren and I have 14.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=9)** - No, no, we'll leave that discussion to another time.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=12)** Instead, let's talk about the investment program you establish for your 14 grandchildren.

>

>

> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=113)** Financial institutions are companies that are experts in managing money, risk, and complicated transactions on behalf of individuals like you and me and companies.

> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=124)** - In essence, I have outsourced this complicated task, managing the saving funds for my 14 grandchildren to an expert to mutual fund company.

>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-financial-institutions?u=76281980&t=142)** - Let's do this, let's study financial institutions.

#### Traditional banks
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=0)** - Financial institutions are companies that are experts in managing money, risk, and complicated transactions on behalf of individuals like you and me, and on behalf of companies.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=13)** There are several different types of financial institutions, ranging from your ordinary local bank to multinational insurance companies.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's first talk about a traditional bank.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=25)** A traditional bank fills the classic role of putting together entrepreneurs with investors/savers.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=32)** The job of a traditional bank is to collect money from you and me, the depositors, hold that money, scan the horizon for potential entrepreneurs who want to borrow that money to use in starting new businesses.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=45)** So, how does a traditional bank make money?
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=48)** Make profits for themselves?
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=50)** Well, look at your bank account.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=52)** What kind of interest rate are you earning on your savings account?
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=56)** These days, I'm earning less than 1% on my deposits of my local bank.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=60)** That's what they're paying me when I deposit money into the bank.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=64)** Okay, so what is my bank charging to business owners who come to the bank and want to borrow money?
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=70)** Well, it depends on the nature of the loan, but I typically don't see business loans with interest rates lower than 5%.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=77)** So, how does a traditional bank make money?
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=79)** It borrows money from me and you, the depositors, and lends that money out at higher rates to entrepreneurs.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=87)** The traditional bank serves the role of a facilitator, a financial facilitator, by taking money from you and me, the savers, and putting that money in the hands of carefully screened entrepreneurs.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=99)** In 2024, the second largest bank in the United States is Bank of America.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=104)** Now, note the largest is J.P. Morgan Chase.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=108)** Bank of America was started in San Francisco by A.P. Giannini back in 1904.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=114)** The original name of the bank? The Bank of Italy.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=117)** Giannini made a reputation for his little bank in 1906 immediately following the devastating San Francisco earthquake.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=126)** In the quake's aftermath, many local business and civic leaders advocated a slow rebuilding with a moratorium on all building loans for six months.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=137)** Giannini disagreed strongly, saying, "Gentlemen, you're making a vital mistake.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=143)** The time for doing business is right now.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=146)** Tomorrow morning, I am putting a desk on Washington Street Wharf with a Bank of Italy sign over it.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=152)** Any man who wants to rebuild San Francisco can come there and get as much cash as he needs to do it."
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=160)** According to a September 30th, 2024 financial report, San Francisco's Bank of Italy descendant, the Bank of America, has total assets of $3.3 trillion.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=173)** Of these assets, a little over $1.1 trillion are traditional loans receivable from banks and individuals.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=181)** The other $2.2 trillion in assets, primarily cash and investments.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=186)** Of the $3.3 trillion in financing needed to buy all these assets, about $1.9 trillion came from deposits from people like you and me.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=195)** Now, in 2024, the second largest bank in Europe was BNP Paribas.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=201)** BNP Paribas was created in 1966 through the merger of two French banks, tracing their roots back to the 1800s.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=209)** According to its September 30th, 2024 financial report, BNP Paribas has total assets of almost €2.8 trillion.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=219)** Of these assets, a little bit over €1.2 trillion are traditional loans receivable from businesses and individuals.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=227)** The other €1.6 trillion in assets, well, like Bank of America, these other assets are primarily cash and investments.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=237)** Of the €2.8 trillion in financing needed to buy all these assets, about €1.5 trillion came from deposits.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=245)** So, from the data of both Bank of America and BNP Paribas, we can see that a primary function of a traditional bank is to collect money from you and me, the depositors, and then lend that money to businesses and individuals.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=261)** But both of these banks also engage in other financial activities, such as investing, providing risk management services, and providing financial advising.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=271)** In the next video, we talk about a class of financial institutions that specialize in providing financial advisory services.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/traditional-banks?u=76281980&t=279)** These are called investment banks.

#### Investment banks

>

#### Investment funds: Index funds
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=0)** - A key aspect of investing is that we can reduce our risk through diversification.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=6)** That is, don't put all your eggs in one basket, but that can be kind of tough.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=11)** Let's say I've got $3,000 to invest.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=13)** It's kind of tough to take that $3,000 and split it up and invest a little bit here and a little bit there and spread it out among 25 or 30 investments.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=23)** An investment fund company helps me do this diversification for a fee.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=29)** So here's what happens with an investment fund.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=31)** First, you send them your money with instructions.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=35)** Here's how I want you to invest my money, then the investment fund, invest the money on my behalf.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=41)** There are different types of investment funds.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=44)** Index funds, managed funds, private equity funds, and hedge funds.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=50)** In this video, we'll focus on index funds.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=53)** Think of an index fund as a computer.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=55)** I send in my $3,000 to the index fund, and I say, "Pick the 500 largest companies in the United States and just spread out my $3,000 investment among those 500 companies."
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=68)** Index funds are very popular because their management fees are very, very low.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=73)** I've seen index funds with fees as low as 0.04% per year.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=80)** Meaning, if you invest $10,000 in an index fund, you're only going to pay $4 to have that $10,000 investment guarded and managed for an entire year.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=93)** Index investing is ideal for people who just want to put their money in and let it sit there growing slowly over time.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=101)** The most popular type of index fund is called the S&P 500 Index.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=107)** S&P 500 stands for Standard and Poor's, which is the name of a company.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=111)** 500 represents the 500 largest companies in the United States.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=116)** The S&P 500 index is a common performance benchmark for all investment funds.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=123)** If I'm managing an investment portfolio, I want to at least beat the S&P 500 index investors.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=130)** Say the following.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=131)** How did the investment manager do compared to the S&P 500 index?
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=136)** If that investment fund can't beat a strategy of just investing in the 500 largest companies in the United States, maybe I should find another fund.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=146)** And investment in the S&P 500 index is not a riskless investment.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=151)** For example, the value of the 500 companies in the index went crazy around the year 2000.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=158)** Before the year 2000, the values went up substantially.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=161)** That was called the internet bubble.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=164)** Not long after the year 2000, the internet bubble burst and the average value of the shares in the companies in the S&P 500 dropped dramatically.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=174)** Before 2008, the S&P 500 index climbed again in association with a housing bubble in the United States.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=181)** When that bubble burst in 2008 and 2009, the S&P 500 index plummeted again.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=188)** And then we experienced the pandemic where the S&P 500 index once again plummeted and then recovered.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-index-funds?u=76281980&t=195)** So an S&P 500 index investment does involve risk, but for investors who are long-term investors, the S&P 500 index can be a very prudent investment over the long term.

#### Investment funds: Managed funds, private-equity funds, and hedge funds
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An alternative to an index fund is a managed fund.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=4)** Think of a managed fund as a room full of experts who spend all day looking at economic news and financial reports for companies and certain industries so that they can then give you an informed investment recommendation.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=19)** In terms of raw performance, you can see that a managed fund has an advantage over an index fund because an index fund is just based on some simple mathematical rule, but the experts who do the stock-picking in a managed fund have to be paid.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=35)** So it's not unusual for a managed fund to have a management fee of 2 or 3% per year.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=42)** With that same $10,000 investment that I can put in an index fund and only pay $4 a year to have it managed, for a managed fund, I might have to pay 200 or $300 per year.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=54)** So there's the trade-off.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=56)** Do I want low management fees with an index fund where I'm not strategically investing my money, or do I want to go with a managed fund?
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=64)** One of the largest index fund investment companies in the world is the Vanguard Group.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=69)** As of May 31st, 2024, Vanguard had $9.3 trillion in assets under management, AMU, a common measure of size among investment fund companies.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=83)** In contrast to a passive index investment strategy such as that made popular by Vanguard is the active investment strategy, involving the informed picking of investments based on analysis, information, and judgment.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=98)** You've also heard of private equity funds.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=101)** Private equity funds are not for you and me.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=104)** These are big investments, say $100 million, or a group of people with lots of savings have put together their money and say, ?We are not going to invest in a few shares of stock.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=115)** We want to buy entire companies."
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=118)** That's what private equity funds do.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=120)** And it's not so easy to get your money out of a private equity fund.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=124)** You have to put your money in with other people, large amounts of money, and you've bought other companies, so it's not easy to pull your money out.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=132)** You may have also heard of hedge funds.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=135)** Think of a hedge fund as a very sophisticated mathematical organization that has done statistical tests and found relations among different market prices.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=145)** For example, a hedge fund might have done an analysis of U.S. dollars, Indonesian rupiah, and stock prices in the Indonesian stock market, and have used those mathematical relations to come up with very sophisticated rapid trading strategies.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=162)** The hedge fund would then invest your money on that basis.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=166)** Investing in hedge funds and private equity funds is not for the fate of heart.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=171)** These investments usually involve large amounts of money.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=175)** Typically, regulations around the world prevent ordinary people who aren't sophisticated investors from getting into hedge fund and private equity investing.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=185)** The most famous investor in the United States is Warren Buffet, CEO of Berkshire Hathaway.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=191)** From 1965 through 2023, an investment in the S&P 500 index earned an average of 10.2% per year.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=202)** By comparison, an investment in Berkshire Hathaway during that same period earned an average of 19.8% per year.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=210)** Notable stock picks made by Warren Buffett includes sizable investments in American Express, Apple, and Coca-Cola.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=218)** Okay, so can you pick investments like Warren Buffett does?
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=222)** Sure, as soon as you have as much information as Warren Buffett has.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=227)** People are always proposing deals to Warren Buffett.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=230)** He considers hundreds of deals per year and invest in only a handful.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=236)** But while considering these proposals, he's given access to all kinds of information.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=241)** So if you are presented with all kinds of extra information, as is Warren Buffett, you too can probably regularly beat the S&P 500 index.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=252)** Now, I'm not here to give investment advice, I'll just speak for myself.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=255)** I don't think I have enough superior information to beat the S&P 500 index.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=260)** So for my personal investing, I just trust that if I spread my money evenly among the 500 largest companies in the United States, I'll probably do okay.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/investment-funds-managed-funds-private-equity-funds-and-hedge-funds?u=76281980&t=270)** I won't do as well as Warren Buffett, but I'll do okay.
