#### Insurance companies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=0)** - The final type of financial institution we want to talk about is insurance companies.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=6)** The social value of insurance is that it shifts risk from ordinary people such as you and me to a large insurance company.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=14)** We pay insurance companies to take risk away from us, healthcare cost risk, house fire risk, automobile damage risk, early death risk, and so forth.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=24)** Insurance companies earn their money in two ways.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=28)** First, they're very good at estimating what their losses will be and are then able to price their premiums to cover those losses.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=35)** That's the most common way we think about insurance companies.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=38)** They charge a premium and the losses they have to pay are less than the premiums that they collect.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=44)** Now what type of insurance companies are there?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=46)** Well, we're familiar with all of these.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=48)** There are property casualty insurance companies.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=50)** They cover losses from flood, fire, earthquake, automobile losses.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=54)** Examples include State Farm, Allstate, and Geico.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=58)** Another type of insurance is health insurance.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=61)** These insurance companies cover traditional health insurance, catastrophic health insurance, disability insurance, and long-term healthcare insurance.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=71)** A well-known health insurance company is Blue Cross Blue Shield.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=76)** There are also life insurance companies.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=78)** You can buy life insurance policies from companies such as Prudential, MetLife, and New York Life.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=85)** We pay premiums and the insurance company guarantees that they will pay money to our family if and when we die.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=92)** Another type of insurance is business insurance.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=95)** Doctors pay insurance companies to help them with medical malpractice.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=100)** Other companies buy business interruption insurance in case some outside event hinders or stops their business operations.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=108)** Companies that ensure businesses include Zurich Insurance, Travelers Insurance, and AIG.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=115)** In addition to balancing the cost of claims against the amount of premiums collected, insurance companies also make money another way, through investments.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=125)** Now think about the timing of the cash flows in the insurance business.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=130)** I pay my premiums to the insurance company now and they pay losses on my behalf later.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=137)** The insurance company has the use of that money from the moment I give it to them to the moment they have to pay out claims.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=144)** What can they do with that float, the money that is available to them?
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=148)** Well, they can invest it in other companies.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=151)** They can invest it in stocks, bonds, and mutual funds.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=154)** They can invest it in real estate development projects.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=157)** Insurance companies make a great deal of money on their investments while they are waiting to pay for the insurance claims that come in.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=165)** Because insurance companies are able to make profits from these investments, they can lower the cost that they charge us for insurance.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=173)** Thus, we see that insurance companies are another form of financial institution.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=178)** To review, if we were all financially sophisticated and had infinite time and perfect communication with everyone in the world, we would arrange borrowing, investing, and savings transactions personally among ourselves.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=193)** We would also manage risk ourselves, we wouldn't need special insurance companies.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=198)** But we don't have infinite time and we don't have perfect information.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=202)** We are not all financially sophisticated.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=205)** Financial institutions provide us with financial sophistication for a price.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=211)** They gather savings from lots of investors like you and me and channel those savings to entrepreneurs on our behalf.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/insurance-companies?u=76281980&t=218)** Four examples of financial institutions are traditional banks, investment banks, investment funds, and insurance companies.

### 8. Capital Budgeting

#### Introducing capital budgeting
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=51)** Will you pay me a billion dollars for it?

> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=61)** You pay me a billion dollars for it now, and you will eventually get your entire billion dollars back.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=67)** - Yeah, but I'll have to wait a billion years.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=69)** Plus a dollar now is worth a lot more than a dollar a billion years from now.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=74)** - Exactly.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=75)** You've hit on the key concept here.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=77)** It's called the Time Value of Money.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=79)** A dollar now is worth more than a dollar to be received five years from now, or 30 years from now, or a billion years from now.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=87)** So when you're evaluating long-term projects, a process called capital budgeting, you have to consider the time value of money.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=96)** - Okay, so what would be a fair price for your billion dollar machine?
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=100)** - Well, let's say an appropriate interest rate is 10%.

> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=103)** Is that okay?

> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-capital-budgeting?u=76281980&t=103)** - Yeah, sure.

#### Capital budgeting overview
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - Capital budgeting is making long-term asset purchase decisions.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=6)** Capital, that's the word we use to refer to the financing obtained by a business in order to buy these long-term assets.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=13)** Budgeting.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=14)** That's numerically planning what we can expect in terms of earning a return on this asset that we're going to buy.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=21)** Making long-term asset purchases involves large amounts of money, so we're talking about a large initial outlay of cash.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=29)** If we make a mistake with these large spending decisions, we could struggle to survive.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=34)** Also, because a capital budgeting purchase locks a company into a long-term asset, there's a potential long-term impact on earnings.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=43)** Long-term assets are with you for a long time.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=46)** Mistakes will punish you year after year.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=50)** Another reason capital budgeting is important is because long-term asset purchases are difficult to reverse.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=57)** For example, assume that we've gone through a capital budgeting process and have decided to construct a technically complex specialized production facility, and then it turns out that a change in circumstances means that we don't need the production facility anymore.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=73)** But now we're stuck with a specialized production facility and it's going to be very difficult to sell.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=80)** The capital budgeting decision could put a drag on our reported net income for years.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=87)** Let me give you an example of the capital budgeting decision process.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=91)** Franklin Noodle Shop is considering the purchase of a new dumpling machine.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=97)** The machine is going to cost $10,000.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=100)** At the end of 10 years, we will be finished using the dumpling machine, and we estimate that we can sell it in the used machine market for a thousand dollars at the end of those 10 years.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=112)** This new dumpling machine is going to reduce our cash operating costs by $700 a year.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=118)** In addition, this new machine will increase our production by 10,000 dumplings a year, and we make 10 cents on each dumpling we sell.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=127)** So that's a $1,000 increase in cash profit each year.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=132)** Finally, our risk adjusted cost of capital is 16%.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=138)** So here's the question.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=139)** Should Franklin Noodle Shop purchase the new dumpling machine?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=144)** Well, let's walk through the cash flows associated with the capital budgeting analysis.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=149)** The original cost is a cash outflow of $10,000 right now.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=155)** In 10 years, we expect to be able to sell the used machines so that that's a cash inflow of $1,000 10 years from now.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=163)** We also expect our cash operating cost to go down by $700 a year.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=168)** Over 10 years, that means we're going to have a total increase in our cash of $7,000 because of reduced cash operating costs.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=177)** We are also going to increase our productive capacity.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=180)** Our cash profits will go up by $1,000 a year for 10 years.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=185)** When I add up the numbers, they look good.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=188)** Minus 10,000, plus 1000 plus 7,000.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=193)** That's $700 a year for 10 years plus 10,000.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=197)** That's $1,000 a year for 10 years, the dumpling machine will give net positive cash flow of $8,000 over the 10 year life of the machine.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=207)** Are we finished?
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=209)** Have we forgotten anything here?
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=212)** Well, what else could we have done with that $10,000 initial investment?
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=218)** Remember that our risk adjusted cost of capital is 16%.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=222)** That can also be thought of as our opportunity rate of return.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=225)** We could have earned 16% a year on other investments.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=231)** So does the net increase of $8,000 over the life of the dumpling machine represent a 16% rate of return?
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=239)** Well, we don't know.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=241)** What have we forgotten here?
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=242)** We have forgotten to include consideration of the time value of money.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=246)** We won't go through the detailed calculations here, but with an interest rate of 16%, the correct decision is to not buy the dumpling machine.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/capital-budgeting-overview?u=76281980&t=256)** In making long-term decisions such as whether to buy this dumpling machine with a life of 10 years, the time value of money is a factor essential to consider.

#### The importance of the time value of money
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about the time value of money.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=4)** Would you prefer to receive $100 today or $100 one year from now?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=9)** What's your preference?
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=10)** I think all of us would prefer to receive $100 today.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=15)** If the interest rate is, for example, 10%, receiving $100 today is the same as receiving $110 a year from now.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=23)** In other words, if I invest that $100 today at 10% a year from now, it will be worth $110.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=31)** So would I take a hundred dollars today or a hundred dollars a year from now?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=35)** I'll take the a hundred dollars today.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=38)** What about taking a hundred dollars today or $110 a year from now?
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=43)** Ah, now we're recognizing that there's a time value to money with a 10% interest rate, receiving $110 one year from now is the same as receiving a hundred dollars today.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=55)** This process is called discounting.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=58)** A hundred dollars today is the equivalent of $110 one year from now.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=62)** If the interest rate's 10%.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=65)** Discounting is the process of explicitly and mathematically using the time value of money to make long term investment decisions.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=75)** We discount all of the cash flows to the present time using the interest rate so that we can compare them all on a common time basis right now.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=85)** In capital budgeting, we estimate what cash flows are going to be in the future, and then we discount those cash flows to right now to see what their cash cashflow equivalent is today.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=96)** Consider, for example, two projects.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=99)** Project A, will generate a single cash inflow of $10,000 at the end of one year.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=105)** Project B, will generate a cash inflow of $50,000 at the end of each year for the next two years.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=112)** Now, which project would you pay more for?
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=115)** A cash inflow of a hundred thousand dollars at the end of one year, or $50,000 at the end of each year for the next two years, assuming an interest rate of 10%.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=125)** Now, keep in mind that both projects involve total cash inflows of a hundred thousand dollars, but those cash inflows are received at different times.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=134)** So let's explicitly consider the time value of money, assuming that the appropriate interest rate is 10%.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=142)** If we're going to receive a hundred thousand dollars one year from now, how much is that worth today?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=147)** Well, we can figure out using a business calculator or an Excel spreadsheet that a hundred thousand dollars to be received a year from now is the equivalent of receiving $90,910 today.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=162)** Another way to think of this is that if I invest, $90,910 today at 10% a year from now, that amount will have grown to a hundred thousand dollars.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=173)** Let's compare project A, which has a present value of $90,910 with Project B, which involves cash flows of $50,000 at the end of each year for the next two years.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=186)** Again, a business calculator or an Excel spreadsheet can be used to compute that the present value of $50,000 each year for two years is only $86,775.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=199)** If the interest rate's 10%, the reason that project B has a lower present value, even though the total cash flow of a hundred thousand dollars is the same as for project A, is that the second $50,000 payment to be received two years from now is not worth as much, because it's further away in time.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-importance-of-the-time-value-of-money?u=76281980&t=217)** The capital budgeting process employs these present value techniques to identify future cash flows and discount them to see what their cash flow equivalent is today.

#### Example: Buying a car in Hong Kong
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's walk through an actual example of a capital budgeting decision.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=5)** This is a real example that my wife Ramona and I went through when we moved to Hong Kong.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=10)** Should we buy a car?
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=12)** The cost of the car we were considering was 40,000 Hong Kong dollars.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, 8 Hong Kong dollars is roughly equivalent to 1 US dollar, so that's about 5,000 US dollars.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=24)** So, so we're not talking about a great car, just a weekend car so we can save on subway and bus fares for my wife and I and our children.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=33)** We figured that our annual savings would be about 10,000 Hong Kong dollars.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=38)** We estimated the use of life of the car to be about five years.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=42)** And for our family cost of capital, we use 10%.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=45)** So, the capital budgeting decision is this.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=48)** Should the Stice family buy this car?
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=51)** Well, the first thing we did was compute the payback period.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=55)** If we spent 40,000 Hong Kong dollars to buy this car and we will save $10,000 per year, we will recover our investment in four years.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=65)** This is called the payback period.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=68)** Well, that made us a little bit nervous because we weren't sure that we would be in Hong Kong longer than three years.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=75)** Now, how would you use that payback period number in a broader example?
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=79)** Let's say that the payback period on the investment in a new computer system is four years.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=84)** You're probably going to be a little bit nervous because the technological life of a computer system these days is just two or three years.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=91)** All right, now let's do some present value calculations to evaluate the Hong Kong car.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=97)** The car costs 40,000 Hong Kong dollars right now.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=102)** The savings will be $10,000 each year for the next five years.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=107)** If the interest rate is 10%, the present value of saving $10,000 each year for five years is $37,908.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=117)** This number can be computed using a business calculator or an Excel spreadsheet.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=123)** Well, computationally, this is a pretty easy decision to make.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=126)** The cost of the car's $40,000.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=129)** If we buy the car, we're going to receive savings that have a value now, a present value of $37,908.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=136)** In other words, we're paying $40,000 to save $37,908.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=143)** Add these two numbers together and you get -$2,092.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=148)** The label for this difference is called the net present value or NPV of the car.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=154)** The NPV of the car is -$2,092.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=159)** The instant that we decide to buy the car, we lose $2,092 in present value terms.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=166)** So, should we buy the car?
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=169)** The payback period analysis says no because the four-year payback period is a significant fraction of the total life of the car, which is five years, and the car has a negative net present value or NPV.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=184)** Now, the worst thing you can say about something in a finance context is that it has a negative net present value.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=191)** If you come up to me and you want to compliment me, you say, "Oh, I was talking to your son the other day and he seems like a positive net present value project."
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=200)** That's the nicest thing you could say to me.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=203)** Now, the numbers say that we should not buy the car, but the numbers are just the start of the analysis.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=209)** We don't have to use our judgment.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=211)** We consider the numbers along with the other non-financial benefits.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=216)** In this case, because of the numbers, my wife and I decided not to buy the car.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=222)** And I think all these years later that we made a mistake.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=227)** We didn't properly wait the non-financial benefits in terms of transportation flexibility to visit the many beautiful places out in the mountains and beaches of Hong Kong.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=238)** We made the decision based on the numbers when we probably should have used a little bit more judgment and considered the non-financial benefits of having a car.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=247)** So remember, when making a capital budgeting decision or any other decision for that matter, the numbers are just the start of the decision making process.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/example-buying-a-car-in-hong-kong?u=76281980&t=257)** Once you've done the numbers, then consider the other non-financial factors.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is called (2), in other words (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), this. (1), case, (1)
> **Env Vars:** npv (3)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

### 9. Advances Using Technology in Finance

#### What is fintech?

>

>

>

#### Blockchain: Introduction
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A blockchain is a distributed data storage system in which data are stored simultaneously on many network computers in time-stamped groups, or blocks, that are linked, or chained, to previously-stored blocks of data.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=19)** Imagine a network of five people who pay money to and collect money from one another on a regular basis.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=27)** Rather than having these transactions go through a traditional bank, the five people decide to take care of the transaction processing, recording, and verification themselves.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=39)** The record of each transaction is put into a computer program that creates a code identifier, a hash for the transaction.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=48)** That hash along with the hash of the immediately preceding transaction is saved along with the transaction data and sent out to the five people in the network as a block of data.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=61)** A collection of blocks of data with each block linked to the preceding block by containing that preceding block's hash, that's called a blockchain.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=71)** These hashes are generated by publicly available cryptographic or code programs.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=77)** This process systematically generates a messy stream of characters and numbers, such as by using certain digits in the square roots and cube roots of prime numbers, that's scary.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=89)** Now, if you give me a hash, can I work backward to get to the original data?
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=96)** Well, yes, but that process basically involves systematically trying every possible initial input to see which one gets transformed into that hash.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=107)** For a detailed record of a financial transaction, working backward through each possibility would require a trillion years or more.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=117)** Here's my favorite description of hashing, unhashing and that process.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=122)** Yes, the process of hashing a cow into ground beef is publicly known and fairly straightforward, but unhashing that process to turn the ground beef back into a cow is not doable in a finite amount of time.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=139)** If an outsider, an evil hacker, gets into one of the computers in the blockchain network and changes a number or character in the transaction data and one of the blocks in the middle of the chain, the hash identifier for that transaction immediately changes.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=156)** Remember that in the network, there are other copies of the original blockchain.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=161)** If the network runs a consensus program, the fact that one copy of the blockchain is corrupted would be quickly revealed.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=169)** In fact, to successfully change any transaction data in the blockchain, a hacker would have to change the data and fix all the hashes in a majority of the computers in the network.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=181)** That is a lot of work.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=183)** This is how data security is maintained in a blockchain system.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=187)** Let's consider how a blockchain system is different from a traditional bank account information system.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=193)** With a traditional bank, the processing and storage of data is centralized.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=198)** Data security is assured through firewalls, encrypted data, careful email policies to keep out hacking attempts on the bank's system, virus and fraud protection software, education of employees and customers and so on.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=213)** Banks basically adopt this policy, put all your eggs in one basket and then carefully protect that basket.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=222)** With a blockchain system, data security is assured through distributed storage and frequent comparison of the verified blocks among the different computers in the network.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=232)** Now, the question is this, when is a blockchain system better than a traditional centralized database system?
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=240)** In the United States, for example, large banks have traditionally proved to be reliable in maintaining accurate financial records for depositors, borrowers, and the transactions with each.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=252)** So continued use of centralized databases by large, trustworthy banks probably makes sense.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=259)** But in a developing economy or in an economy where there has just been a major financial meltdown, a blockchain system makes more sense because responsibility for maintaining the database is shared among the network of users.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=274)** Blockchain technology is sometimes described as the solution to all of our data problems.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/blockchain-introduction?u=76281980&t=281)** While that's true in many cases, but in settings in which the central organization, such as the bank, is trusted and has reliable redundancies in terms of data storage and backup facilities, there's still room for centralized data systems.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Analogies:** such as (2), imagine (1), for example (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), let (1), this, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Cryptocurrency
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=0)** - So, what exactly is cryptocurrency?
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=4)** I'm sure you've heard of it.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=6)** And many, when they hear of it, nod their head like they know what it is.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=11)** And then inside themselves, they say, "I don't know what it is."
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=14)** Well, let's see if we can take away some of the mystery.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=17)** Cryptocurrency is a decentralized electronic currency whose existence and ownership are documented through cryptographically secured data in a blockchain.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=29)** Now, here are three necessary characteristics of a reliable currency.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=34)** Reasonable limit on supply of the currency, verifiable, and broadly accepted.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=41)** Now, before we get to cryptocurrency, let's think about each of these three characteristics with respect to gold and to United States $100 bills.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=50)** First, is there a reasonable limit on supply?
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=53)** Well, gold is tough to mine.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=56)** I myself have been two miles down in a gold mine in South Africa.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=61)** The world is not going to be suddenly flooded with extra gold.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, with $100 bills and all other forms of US money, the supply is limited by the policies of the US Central Bank, the Federal Reserve.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=74)** If we trust the Federal Reserve to set good policies, then US currency is a reliable currency.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=81)** Next, verifiable.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=84)** Now, there are chemical tests that can confirm the quality of a gold bar or a gold coin.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=89)** US currency is created with several embedded security features.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=93)** You may have seen a wary cashier hold your $100 bill up to an ultraviolet lamp.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=99)** The UV lamp reveals ink visible only under UV light.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=104)** Next, broadly accepted.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=107)** Well, I know from personal experience, a crisp new $100 bill is accepted just about everywhere in the world.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=114)** Now, I have never tried to buy anything with gold, but my brother, who has some experience with gold, tells me that reputable gold dealers are everywhere.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=125)** Okay, now that brings us to cryptocurrency.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=128)** Is there a reasonable limit on the supply?
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=130)** Is it verifiable?
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=132)** Is it broadly accepted?
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=134)** Well, my discussion here is based on my understanding of Bitcoin, which is the original and most widely used cryptocurrency.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=142)** Bitcoin is verifiable because it's based on the blockchain technology that we discussed in the previous video.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=149)** Starting with the first 50 Bitcoins created on January 3rd, 2009, the Bitcoin blockchain contains a record of every Bitcoin transaction since then.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=160)** And 'cause this blockchain is duplicated in thousands of widely dispersed nodes, corrupting it is practically impossible.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=169)** The sender and receiver of a Bitcoin transaction have private and public keys that allow them to securely exchange transaction information.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=178)** With Bitcoin, these private and public keys are used with the SHA-256 algorithm to create easily decodable transaction data for those with the private keys.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=191)** Note, detailed discussion of private keys, public keys, and secured transaction transmission are outside the scope of this course.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=201)** Now, before a new transaction is added to the Bitcoin blockchain, it must be validated.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=206)** The validators are called miners.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=209)** A miner runs a fast computer program that uses the hash associated with the transaction to create a new hash having characteristics defined by the system.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=220)** Creating the new hash involves very fast guessing.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=224)** The miners have an incentive to do this because the first miner to generate an appropriate hash gets a reward, which is hundreds of thousands of dollars.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=234)** So because of the cryptographic protection of a blockchain and because of competitive validation by the miners, Bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies are verifiable.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=246)** Next, is there a reasonable limit on the supply of Bitcoin?
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=250)** Yep.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=251)** The Bitcoin system is capped at 21 million Bitcoins, and as time goes by, the system is set up to make Bitcoin mining progressively more difficult.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=262)** Finally, is Bitcoin widely accepted?
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=265)** Accepted, yes.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=267)** Widely, not yet.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=270)** So far, few regular businesses accept Bitcoin, but I read that McDonald's is experimenting with accepting Bitcoin at a few of its locations outside the United States, and you can even go today and pay for a luxury car purchase using Bitcoin.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=287)** Cryptocurrencies, such as Bitcoin, are developing all the characteristics of a genuine currency.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/cryptocurrency?u=76281980&t=293)** Verifiable, limited supply, and increasingly broad acceptance.

#### Alternative financing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=0)** - Alternative financing mechanisms such as peer-to-peer loans, crowdfunding, and loans obtained through non-traditional FinTech companies can be a threat to traditional financing mechanisms, but for now, only for the financing of small companies.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, here's a story that illustrates this potential threat.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=21)** A few years back, I was standing in the lobby of a bank waiting my turn.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=27)** On the wall to my right, I saw a big poster advertising the bank's business loan rates.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=33)** "Business loans for as low as 6%." The poster said.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=37)** On the wall to my left, I saw another big poster.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=41)** "Attractive rates for depositors!
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=42)** We are paying rates as high as 1.2%!"
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=46)** 6%.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=48)** 1.2%.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=49)** As I stood there in line, I thought, "What if the bank's depositors making 1.2% and the bank's borrowers expected to pay at least 6%, what if they started talking to each other in this very line?"
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=62)** The depositor could get a higher rate and the borrower could pay a lower rate if they went out into the bank's parking lot and negotiated directly between themselves.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=72)** Well, that's exactly what online loan markets do.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=77)** One leading peer-to-peer lending company is called Prosper.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=81)** Prosper has been around since 2005, and as of 2025, has arranged loans of over $27 billion for 1.7 million borrowers.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=93)** Loan amounts range from 2,000 to $40,000.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=98)** So these loans are targeted at individuals and small business owners.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=103)** Prosper requires a potential borrower to use an online form to provide a few financial numbers such as current income, existing loans, and the purpose of getting this new loan.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=114)** Using these data, Prosper's system evaluates the riskiness of the loan and then reports what the loan interest rate will be.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=123)** Prosper loan rates range from 7% to very creditworthy individuals to as high as 36% for loans to higher-risk individuals.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=133)** Investors who want to use the Prosper system to make loans choose from among as yet unfunded loans listed by risk category, so the investor can make low-risk loans to safe individuals or can seek higher returns by making loans to high-risk individuals.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=152)** A Prosper investor can get started with as little as $25 to partially fund an available loan.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=160)** A peer-to-peer business loan is appropriate for a small business owner who does not feel the need for the business advice that is often part of the loan process with a bank or who wants to streamline the loan process.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=175)** There are other non-bank sources for loans.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=177)** For example, you can get a business loan through PayPal.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=181)** The advantage of such non-traditional loans is they can be approved very quickly, as soon as the next business day.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=188)** The significant disadvantage is that effective annual interest rates, including fees, on these loans can be very high, sometimes over 100%.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=199)** Okay, what about equity funding?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=201)** Equity funding is financing raised from people who then want to become part owners of the business, not a loan, but in investment.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=210)** Well, first note that I'm not talking about charitable funding efforts such as GoFundMe, and I'm also not talking about contributions to a new company, perhaps in exchange for a good deal on the purchase of the company's product or service.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=224)** This is how Kickstarter works.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=226)** Instead, I'm talking about people investing in small businesses to earn returns from ownership.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=233)** For large companies, this process involves raising seed money from initial angel investors, then more financing from venture capital investors, and then perhaps, finally, an initial public offering, an IPO, on a stock exchange.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=248)** Equity crowdfunding involves an online platform through which lots of small investors can purchase ownership shares in a small business.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=258)** Wefunder is an equity crowdfunding service that, as of February 2025, had matched over 1 million investors with 3,607 company founders, directly resulting in $865 million in funding.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=276)** Wefunder reports that you can invest as little as $100 in startup companies with business plans ranging from coffee shops to flying cars.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=286)** But equity crowdfunding is not for nervous investors.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=290)** Wefunder gives this advice: "Startup investing is very risky.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=296)** You can win big or lose it all, so don't invest more than you can afford to lose."
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/alternative-financing?u=76281980&t=302)** In summary, peer-to-peer loans and equity crowdfunding are alternate ways for small businesses to obtain financing without using traditional financial institutions.

### 10. Personal Financial Planning

#### Introducing personal financing planning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=0)** - All right, you've been around for a while.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=2)** You've seen get-rich-quick schemes come and go.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=5)** - Yes, I have.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=6)** - So tell us about your favorite one.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=8)** By favorite, I mean the one that you thought was the most ridiculous.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=12)** - All right, well, it was called, let's not use the real name, but here was the idea.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=18)** You pay $6,000 to become a member, you then commit to visiting 12 websites per day.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=24)** Now, these websites are supposedly from customers who are paying for an increase in traffic to their website.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=31)** Visiting these 12 websites per day takes about five minutes.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=35)** Each day you do this, you earn 12% of your initial membership fee, or $720.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=42)** You do this every day for 12 days.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=45)** So at the end of 12 days, you receive a total of $8,640.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=51)** Subtract your initial investment of $6,000 and you've earned $2,640 for 12 days of work, spending five minutes each day visiting 12 websites.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=62)** - Okay, so what's wrong with this?
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=65)** - Well, the whole thing was bogus.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=66)** Is it reasonable to think that you can make $720 each day by spending five minutes visiting websites?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=74)** - You're right.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=75)** That is ridiculous.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=76)** So how was this scam able to attract members?
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=79)** - Well, it was a classic Ponzi scheme, meaning that old members were paid off using the membership fees of new members.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=87)** There was no outside money.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=89)** There were no real website customers.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=92)** - So did anybody really fall for this?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=95)** - Absolutely.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=96)** In fact, you and I both know someone who lost a few thousand dollars in this scam.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=101)** - Who was that?
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=102)** - Well, I'm not going to tell you on camera.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=103)** I'll tell you later.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=104)** People, even people that you know, get caught up in the excitement of get-rich-quick schemes that are too good to be true.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=112)** In fact, if a financial plan seems too good to be true, then it probably is too good to be true.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=118)** - Real financial planning is simple.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=121)** In fact, the backbone of personal financial planning is this simple commitment.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=126)** Spend less than you make.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/introducing-personal-financing-planning?u=76281980&t=128)** - Let's talk about some basic elements of personal financial planning.

#### The secret to financial security
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/the-secret-to-financial-security?u=76281980)

#### Simple savings and investment plans

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about personal savings and investment plans.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=4)** Here's the key concept.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=6)** If you're considering a personal savings or investment plan and it seems clever or complex, it's almost certainly a bad idea.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=15)** Here is the kind of investment plan that you need.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, this comes from Dave Ramsey's Seven Baby Steps that he explains in his website, [daveramsey.com](https://daveramsey.com).
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=23)** First, get a thousand dollars and put it in an emergency fund.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=28)** Nowhere fancy.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=29)** Just take it down to your bank and put it there.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=32)** Life's troubles seem a lot easier to handle if you've got a thousand dollars in an emergency fund.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=37)** Next, start paying off your debts, starting with the smallest ones because then you can build some momentum.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=44)** So after steps one and two, you've got a thousand dollars in the bank and you've paid off your debts.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=50)** Next, build up three to six months of living expenses in savings in that same bank account.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=57)** Nothing fancy.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=59)** People who have three to six months of living expenses in savings have lots of financial flexibility and lots of peace of mind.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=68)** Now you've done steps one, two, and three, step four is to seriously start saving for the long term, such as long-term retirement savings.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=79)** Now, you want to take advantage of individual retirement accounts and the 401k plan at work.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=85)** Now you're saying to yourself, "Yeah, but if I do this, I'm not going to get rich quick."
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=89)** That's right.

> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=90)** You're not going to get rich quick.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=92)** A few people who get rich get rich through a combination of luck and possessing some special skill or product, but most of us aren't this lucky.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=101)** Long, steady savings is how most people get rich.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=106)** Long-term investing should be in some kind of diversified fund, such as an S&P 500 index fund.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=113)** Now, if you want to earn next month's rent, get a job.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=116)** Investing in the stock market should be for the long term, a 20- to 30-year horizon.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=123)** Now, eventually somebody's going to come to you and say, "Listen, I've got this investment plan that will guarantee you high returns, 30% per year guaranteed."
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=132)** If anybody ever says that to you, you run away from that person as fast as you can.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=137)** That person is either a fool, meaning they don't know what they're talking about, or they're trying to trick you.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=143)** Either way, you don't want anything to do with them.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=145)** High returns are possible, but they can never be guaranteed.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=150)** Investing involves short-term risk, so the best approach to investing is to have a long-term planning horizon.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=158)** With respect to investments and savings, beware of anything clever or complex.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/simple-savings-and-investment-plans?u=76281980&t=165)** Simple savings, starting with a bank account and working your way up, that's your best bet.

### Conclusion

#### Reviewing corporate finance
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=1)** - Let's review what we've talked about in this finance course.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=4)** - The first thing we did was introduce the topic of finance by addressing the key question, what is finance?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=9)** - [Instructor] Finance is deciding what you should buy, how you should get the money to buy it, and how you should manage it once you have it.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=16)** Then we talked about the financial statements, the balance sheet, the income statement, and the statement of cash flows.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=22)** - We discussed short-term financial management, which is monitoring and controlling the cash flows in and out of a business, monitoring the receivables, the inventory, and the payables.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=34)** - Then we talked about the important trade-off between risk and return.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=38)** - We talked about capital structure.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=41)** What is the optimal way, the most value increasing way to finance my operations.

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/finance-foundations-24834241/reviewing-corporate-finance?u=76281980&t=63)** - And we ended with a short discussion of personal financial planning, which is really quite simple.
